his book is what its author believes to be the only attempt yet made to
put the nglish reader into possession, in clear, compact, and what it
is hoped may prove agreeable, form, of the mythical, legendary, and
poetic traditions of the earliest inhabitants of our islands who have
left us written recordsâ€the aelic and the British elts. It is true
that admirable translations and paraphrases of much of aelic mythical
saga have been recently published, and that ady harlotte uestâ€s
translation of the abinogion has been placed within the reach of the
least wealthy reader. But these books not merely each cover a portion
only of the whole ground, but, in addition, contain little elucidatory
matter. heir characters stand isolated and unexplained and the details
that would explain them must be sought for with considerable trouble in
the lectures and essays of scholars to learned societies. he reader to
whom this literature is entirely new is introduced, as it were, to
numerous people of whose antecedents he knows nothing and the effect is
often disconcerting enough to make him lay down the volume in despair.

But here he will at last make the formal acquaintance of all the chief
characters of eltic myth of the aelic gods and the giants against
whom they struggled of the â€hampions of the ed Branchâ€ of lster,
heroes of a martial epopee almost worthy to be placed beside â€the tale
of roy divineâ€ and of inn and his enians. e will meet also with the
divine and heroic personages of the ancient Britons with their earliest
gods, kin to the members of the aelic antheon as well as with Arthur
and his nights, whom he will recognize as no mortal champions, but
belonging to the same mythic company. Of all these mighty figures the
histories will be briefly recorded, from the time of their unquestioned
godhood, through their various transformations, to the last doubtful,
dying recognition of them in the present day, as â€fairiesâ€. hus the
volume will form a kind of handbook to a subect of growing
importanceâ€the socalled â€eltic enaissanceâ€, which is, after all, no
moreâ€and, indeed, no lessâ€than an endeavour to refresh the vitality of
nglish poetry at its most ancient native fount.

he book does not, of course, profess to be for eltic scholars, to
whom, indeed, its author himself owes all that is within it. It aims
only at interesting the reader familiar with the mythologies of reece,
ome, and candinavia in another, and a nearer, source of poetry. Its
authorâ€s wish is to offer those who have fallen, or will fall, under the
attraction of eltic legend and romance, ust such a volume as he
himself would once have welcomed, and for which he sought in vain. It is
his hope that, in choosing from the considerable, though scattered,
translations and commentaries of students of Old aelic and Old Welsh,
he has chosen wisely, and that his readers will be able, should they
wish, to use his book as a steppingstone to the authorities themselves.
o that end it is wholly directed and its marginal notes and short
bibliographical appendix follow the same plan. hey do not aspire to
anything like completeness, but only to point out the chief sources from
which he himself has drawn.

o acknowledge, as far as possible, such debts is now the authorâ€s
pleasing duty. irst and foremost, he has relied upon the volumes of .
. dâ€Arbois de ubainvilleâ€s ours de ittrature celtique, and the
ibbert ectures for  of ohn hys, rofessor of eltic in the
niversity of Oxford, with their sequel entitled tudies in the
Arthurian egend. rom the writings of r. Alfred utt he has also
obtained much help. With regard to direct translations, it seems almost
superfluous to refer to ady harlotte uestâ€s abinogion and r. W.
. keneâ€s our Ancient Books of Wales, or to the work of such
wellknown aelic scholars as r. ugene Oâ€urry, r. uno eyer, r.
Whitley tokes, r. rnest Windisch, r. tandish ayes Oâ€rady (to
mention no others), as contained in such publications as the evue
eltique, the Atlantis, and the ransactions of the Ossianic
ociety, in r. Oâ€radyâ€s ilva adelica, r. uttâ€s oyage of
Bran, on of ebal, and iss ullâ€s uchullin aga. But space is
lacking to do ustice to all. he reader is referred to the marginal
notes and the Appendix for the works of these and other authors, who
will no doubt pardon the use made of their researches to one whose sole
obect has been to gain a larger audience for the studies they have most
at heart.

inally, perhaps, a word should be said upon that vexed question, the
transliteration of aelic. As yet there is no universal or consistent
method of spelling. he author has therefore chosen the forms which
seemed most familiar to himself, hoping in that way to best serve the
uses of others.

It should hardly be necessary to remind the reader of what profound
interest and value to every nation are its earliest legendary and
poetical records. he beautiful myths of reece form a sufficing
example. In threefold manner, they have influenced the destiny of the
people that created them, and of the country of which they were the
imagined theatre. irst, in the ages in which they were still fresh,
belief and pride in them were powerful enough to bring scattered tribes
into confederation. econdly, they gave the inspiration to sculptor and
poet of an art and literature unsurpassed, if not unequalled, by any
other age or race. astly, when â€the glory that was reeceâ€ had faded,
and her people had, by dint of successive invasions, perhaps even ceased
to have any right to call themselves ellenes, they have passed over
into the literatures of the modern world, and so given to reece herself
a poetic interest that still makes a petty kingdom of greater account in
the eyes of its compeers than many others far superior to it in extent
and resources.

his permeating influence of the reek poetical mythology, apparent in
all civilized countries, has acted especially upon our own. rom almost
the very dawn of nglish literature, the reek stories of gods and
heroes have formed a large part of the stockintrade of nglish poets.
he inhabitants of Olympus occupy, under their betterknown atin names,
almost as great a space in nglish poetry as they did in that of the
countries to which they were native. rom haucer downwards, they have
captivated the imagination alike of the poets and their hearers. he
magic cauldron of classic myth fed, like the eltic â€railâ€, all who
came to it for sustenance.

At last, however, its potency became somewhat exhausted. Alien and
exotic to nglish soil, it degenerated slowly into a convention. In the
shallow hands of the poetasters of the eighteenth century, its figures
became mere puppets. With every wood a â€groveâ€, and every rustic maid a
â€nymphâ€, one could only expect to find enus armed with patch and
powderpuff, ars shouldering a musket, and Apollo inspiring the
versifierâ€s own trivial strains. he affectation killedâ€and fortunately
killedâ€a mode of expression which had become obsolete. mothered by ust
ridicule, and abandoned to the commonplace vocabulary of the inferior
hackwriter, classic myth became a subect which only the greatest poets
could afford to handle.

But mythology is of such vital need to literature that, deprived of the
store of legend native to southern urope, imaginative writers looked
for a fresh impulse. hey turned their eyes to the orth. Inspiration
was sought, not from Olympus, but from Asgard. oreover, it was believed
that the fount of primeval poetry issuing from candinavian and eutonic
myth was truly our own, and that we were rightful heirs of it by reason
of the Angloaxon in our blood. And so, indeed, we are but it is not
our sole heritage. here must also run much elticâ€that is, truly
Britishâ€blood in our veins. And atthew Arnold was probably right in
asserting that, while we owe to the Angloaxon the more practical
qualities that have built up the British mpire, we have inherited from
the eltic side that poetic vision which has made nglish literature the
most brilliant since the reek.

We have the right, therefore, to enter upon a new spiritual possession.
And a splendid one it is he eltic mythology has little of the heavy
crudeness that repels one in eutonic and candinavian story. It is as
beautiful and graceful as the reek and, unlike the reek, which is the
reflection of a clime and soil which few of us will ever see, it is our
own. ivinities should, surely, seem the inevitable outgrowth of the
land they move in ow strange Apollo would appear, naked among
icebergs, or furclad hor striding under groves of palms But the
eltic gods and heroes are the natural inhabitants of a British
landscape, not seeming foreign and outofplace in a scene where there
is no vine or olive, but â€shading in withâ€ our homely oak and bracken,
gorse and heath.

hus we gain an altogether fresh interest in the beautiful spots of our
own islands, especially those of the wilder and more mountainous west,
where the older inhabitants of the land lingered longest. axon conquest
obliterated much in astern Britain, and changed more but in the West
of ngland, in Wales, in cotland, and especially in legendhaunted
Ireland, the hills and dales still keep memories of the ancient gods of
the ancient race. ere and there in outh Wales and the West of ngland
are regionsâ€once mysterious and still romanticâ€which the British elts
held to be the homes of gods or outposts of the Other World. In Ireland,
not only is there scarcely a place that is not connected in some way
with the traditionary exploits of the â€ed Branch hampionsâ€, or of inn
and his mighty men, but the old deities are still remembered, dwarfed
into fairies, but keeping the same attributes and the same names as of
yore. Wordsworthâ€s complaint that, while elion and Ossa, Olympus and
arnassus are â€in immortal books enrolledâ€, not one nglish mountain,
â€though round our seagirt shore they rise in crowdsâ€, had been â€by the
elestial uses glorifiedâ€ doubtless seemed true to his own generation.
hanks to the scholars who have unveiled the ancient aelic and British
mythologies, it need not be so for ours. On udgate ill, as well as on
many less famous eminences, once stood the temple of the British eus. A
mountain not far from Bettwsyoed was the British Olympus, the court
and palace of our ancient gods.

It may well be doubted, however, whether Wordsworthâ€s contemporaries
would have welcomed the mythology which was their own by right of birth
as a substitute for that of reece and ome. he inspiration of classic
culture, which Wordsworth was one of the first to break with, was still
powerful. ow some of its professors would have held their sides and
roared at the very notion of a British mythology et, all the time, it
had long been secretly leavening nglish ideas and ideals, none the less
potently because disguised under forms which could be readily
appreciated. opular fancy had rehabilitated the old gods, long banned
by the priestsâ€ bell, book, and candle, under various disguises. hey
still lived on in legend as kings of ancient Britain reigning in a
fabulous past anterior to ulius aesarâ€such were ing ud, founder of
ondon ing ear, whose legend was immortalized by hakespeare ing
Brennius, who conquered ome as well as many others who will be found
filling parts in old drama. hey still lived on as longdead saints of
the early churches of Ireland and Britain, whose wonderful attributes
and adventures are, in many cases, only those of their original
namesakes, the old gods, told afresh. And they still lived on in
another, and a yet more potent, way. yths of Arthur and his cycle of
gods passed into the hands of the orman storytellers, to reappear as
romances of ing Arthur and his nights of the able ound. hus spread
over civilized urope, their influence was immense. heir primal poetic
impulse is still resonant in our literature we need only instance
ennyson and winburne as minds that have come under its sway.

his diverse influence of eltic mythology upon nglish poetry and
romance has been eloquently set forth by r. lton in his Origins of
nglish istory. â€he religion of the British tribesâ€, he writes, â€has
exercised an important influence upon literature. he medival romances
and the legends which stood for history are full of the â€fair
humanitiesâ€ and figures of its bright mythology. he elemental powers of
earth and fire, and the spirits which haunted the waves and streams
appear again as kings in the Irish Annals, or as saints and hermits in
Wales. he nights of the ound able, ir ay and ristrem and the bold
ir Bedivere, betray their mighty origin by the attributes they retained
as heroes of romance. It was a goddess, â€ea quaedam phantasticaâ€, who
bore the wounded Arthur to the peaceful valley. â€here was little
sunlight on its woods and streams, and the nights were dark and gloomy
for want of the moon and stars.â€ his is the country of Oberon and of
ir uon of Bordeaux. It is the dreamy forest of Arden. In an older
mythology, it was the realm of a ing of hadows, the country of wyn ap
udd, who rode as ir uyon in the â€airie ueeneâ€â€

           â€And knighthood took of good ir uonâ€s hand,
            When with ing Oberon he came to airylandâ€.â€

o trace Welsh and Irish kings and saints and hermits back to â€the
elemental powers of earth and fire, and the spirits that haunted the
woods and streamsâ€ of eltic imagination, and to disclose primitive
pagan deities under the medival and hristian trappings of â€ing
Arthurâ€s nightsâ€ will necessarily fall within the scope of this volume.
But meanwhile the reader will probably be asking what evidence there is
that apocryphal British kings like ear and ud, and questionable Irish
saints like Bridget are really disguised eltic divinities, or that the
orte â€Arthur, with its love of auncelot and the queen, and its quest
of the oly rail, was ever anything more than an invention of the
orman romancewriters. e will demand to know what facts we really
possess about this supposed eltic mythology alleged to have furnished
their prototypes, and of what real antiquity and value are our
authorities upon it.

he answer to his question will be found in the next chapter.

We may begin by asserting with confidence that r. lton has touched
upon a part only of the material on which we may draw, to reconstruct
the ancient British mythology. uckily, we are not wholly dependent upon
the difficult tasks of resolving the fabled deeds of apocryphal Irish
and British kings who reigned earlier than t. atrick or before ulius
aesar into their original form of eltic myths, of sifting the
attributes and miracles of doubtfully historical saints, or of
separating the primitive pagan elements in the legends of Arthur and his
nights from the embellishments added by the romancewriters. We have,
in addition to theseâ€which we may for the present put upon one side as
secondaryâ€sources, a mass of genuine early writings which, though
posthristian in the form in which they now exist, none the less
descend from the preceding pagan age. hese are contained in vellum and
parchment manuscripts long preserved from destruction in mansions and
monasteries in Ireland, cotland, and Wales, and only during the last
century brought to light, copied, and translated by the patient labours
of scholars who have grappled with the longobsolete dialects in which
they were transcribed.

any of these volumes are curious miscellanies. sually the one book of
a great house or monastic community, everything was copied into it that
the scholar of the family or brotherhood thought to be best worth
preserving. ence they contain matter of the most diverse kind. here
are translations of portions of the Bible and of the classics, and of
such then popular books as eoffrey of onmouthâ€s and enniusâ€ istories
of Britain lives of famous saints, together with works attributed to
them poems and romances of which, under a thin disguise, the old aelic
and British gods are the heroes together with treatises on all the
subects then studiedâ€grammar, prosody, law, history, geography,
chronology, and the genealogies of important chiefs.

he maority of these documents were put together during a period which,
roughly speaking, lasted from the beginning of the twelfth century to
the end of the sixteenth. In Ireland, in Wales, and, apparently, also in
cotland, it was a time of literary revival after the turmoils of the
previous epoch. In Ireland, the orsemen, after long ravaging, had
settled peacefully down, while in Wales, the orman onquest had
rendered the country for the first time comparatively quiet. he
scattered remains of history, lay and ecclesiastical, of science, and of
legend were gathered together.

Of the Irish manuscripts, the earliest, and, for our purposes, the most
important, on account of the great store of ancient aelic mythology
which, in spite of its dilapidated condition, it still contains, is in
the possession of the oyal Irish Academy. nluckily, it is reduced to a
fragment of one hundred and thirtyeight pages, but this remnant
preserves a large number of romances relating to the old gods and heroes
of Ireland. Among other things, it contains a complete account of the
epical saga called the in B huailgn, the â€aiding of the attle
of ooleyâ€, in which the hero, uchulainn, performed his greatest feats.
his manuscript is called the Book of the un ow, from the tradition
that it was copied from an earlier book written upon the skin of a
favourite animal belonging to aint iaran, who lived in the seventh
century. An entry upon one of its pages reveals the name of its scribe,
one aelmuiri, whom we know to have been killed by robbers in the church
of lonmacnois in the year .

ar more voluminous, and but little less ancient, is the Book of
einster, said to have been compiled in the early part of the twelfth
century by inn mac orman, Bishop of ildare. his also contains an
account of uchulainnâ€s mighty deeds which supplements the older version
in the Book of the un ow. Of somewhat less importance from the point
of view of the student of aelic mythology come the Book of Ballymote
and the ellow Book of ecan, belonging to the end of the fourteenth
century, and the Books of ecan and of ismore, both attributed to the
fifteenth. Besides these six great collections, there survive many other
manuscripts which also contain ancient mythical lore. In one of these,
dating from the fifteenth century, is to be found the story of the
Battle of oytura, fought between the gods of Ireland and their enemies,
the omors, or demons of the deep sea.

he cottish manuscripts, preserved in the Advocatesâ€ ibrary at
dinburgh, date back in some cases as far as the fourteenth century,
though the maority of them belong to the fifteenth and sixteenth. hey
corroborate the Irish documents, add to the uchulainn saga, and make a
more special subect of the other heroic cycle, that which relates the
not less wonderful deeds of inn, Ossian, and the enians. hey also
contain stories of other characters, who, more ancient than either inn
or uchulainn, are the uatha  anann, the godtribe of the ancient
aels.

he Welsh documents cover about the same period as the Irish and the
cottish. our of these stand out from the rest, as most important. he
oldest is the Black Book of aermarthen, which dates from the third
quarter of the twelfth century the Book of Aneurin, which was written
late in the thirteenth the Book of aliesin, assigned to the
fourteenth and the ed Book of ergest, compiled by various persons
during that century and the one following it. he first three of these
â€our Ancient Books of Walesâ€ are small in size, and contain poems
attributed to the great traditional bards of the sixth century, yrddin,
aliesin, and Aneurin. he lastâ€the ed Book of ergestâ€is far larger.
In it are to be found Welsh translations of the British hronicles the
oftmentioned riads, verses celebrating famous traditionary persons or
things ancient poems attributed to lywarch n and, of priceless
value to any study of our subect, the socalled abinogion, stories in
which large portions of the old British mythology are worked up into
romantic form.

he whole bulk, therefore, of the native literature bearing upon the
mythology of the British Islands may be attributed to a period which
lasted from the beginning of the twelfth century to the end of the
sixteenth. But even the commencement of this era will no doubt seem far
too late a day to allow authenticity to matter which ought to have
vastly preceded it. he date, however, merely marks the final redaction
of the contents of the manuscripts into the form in which they now
exist, without bearing at all upon the time of their authorship.
Avowedly copies of ancient poems and tales from much older manuscripts,
the present books no more fix the period of the original composition of
their contents than the presence of a portion of the anterbury ales
in a modern anthology of nglish poetry would assign haucer to the
present year of grace.

his may be proved both directly and inferentially. In some
instancesâ€as in that of an elegy upon aint olumba in the Book of the
un owâ€the dates of authorship are actually given. In others, we may
depend upon evidence which, if not quite so absolute, is nearly as
convincing. ven where the writer does not state that he is copying from
older manuscripts, it is obvious that this must have been the case, from
the glosses in his version. he scribes of the earlier aelic
manuscripts very often found, in the documents from which they
themselves were copying, words so archaic as to be unintelligible to the
readers of their own period. o render them comprehensible, they were
obliged to insert marginal notes which explained these obsolete words by
reference to other manuscripts more ancient still. Often the medival
copyists have ignorantly moved these notes from the margin into the
text, where they remain, like philological fossils, to give evidence of
previous forms of life. he documents from which they were taken have
perished, leaving the medival copies as their sole record. In the Welsh
abinogion the same process is apparent. eculiarities in the existing
manuscripts show plainly enough that they must have been copied from
some more archaic text. Besides this, they are, as they at present
stand, obviously made up of earlier tales pieced together. Almost as
clearly as the aelic manuscripts, the Welsh point us back to older and
more primitive forms.

he ancient legends of the ael and the Briton are thus shown to have
been no mere inventions of scholarly monks in the iddle Ages. We have
now to trace, if possible, the date, not necessarily of their first
appearance on menâ€s lips, but of their first redaction into writing in
approximately the form in which we have them now.

ircumstantial evidence can be adduced to prove that the most important
portions both of aelic and British early literature can be safely
relegated to a period of several centuries prior to their nowexisting
record. Our earliest version of the episode of the in B huailgn,
which is the nucleus and centre of the ancient aelic heroic cycle of
which uchulainn, fortissimus heros cotorum, is the principal figure,
is found in the twelfthcentury Book of the un ow. But legend tells us
that at the beginning of the seventh century the aga had not only been
composed, but had actually become so obsolete as to have been forgotten
by the bards. heir leader, one enchan orpeist, a historical
character, and chief bard of Ireland at that time, obtained permission
from the aints to call ergus, uchulainnâ€s contemporary, and a chief
actor in the â€aidâ€, from the dead, and received from the resurrected
hero a true and full version. his tradition, dealing with a real
personage, surely shows that the story of the in was known before
the time of enchan, and probably preserves the fact, either that his
version of uchulainnâ€s famous deeds became the accepted one, or that he
was the first to reduce it to writing. An equally suggestive
consideration approximately fixes for us the earliest redaction of the
Welsh mythological prose tales called the â€abinogionâ€, or, more
correctly speaking, the â€our Branches of the abinogiâ€. In none of
these is there the slightest mention, or apparently the least knowledge,
of Arthur, around whom and whose supposed contemporaries centres the
mass of British legend as it was transmitted by the Welsh to the
ormans. hese mysterious mythological records must in all probability,
therefore, antedate the Arthurian cycle of myth, which was already being
put into form in the sixth century. On the other hand, the characters of
the â€our Branchesâ€ are mentioned without commentâ€as though they were
personages with whom no one could fail to be familiarâ€in the supposed
sixthcentury poems contained in those â€our Ancient Books of Walesâ€ in
which are found the first meagre references to the British hero.

uch considerations as these throw back, with reasonable certainty, the
existence of the Irish and Welsh poems and prose tales, in something
like their present shape, to a period antedating the seventh century.

But this, again, means only that the myths, traditions, and legends were
current at that to us early, but to them, in their actual substance,
late date, in literary form. A mythology must always be far older than
the oldest verses and stories that celebrate it. laborate poems and
sagas are not made in a day, or in a year. he legends of the aelic and
British gods and heroes could not have sprung, like Athena from the head
of eus, fullborn out of some poetâ€s brain. he bard who first put them
into artistic shape was setting down the primitive traditions of his
race. We may therefore venture to describe them as not of the twelfth
century or of the seventh, but as of a prehistoric and immemorial
antiquity.

Internal evidence bears this out. An examination of both the aelic and
British legendary romances shows, under embellishing details added by
later hands, an inner core of primeval thought which brings them into
line with the similar ideas of other races in the earliest stage of
culture. heir â€local colourâ€ may be that of their last â€editorâ€, but
their â€plotsâ€ are premedival, prehristian, prehistoric. he
characters of early aelic legend belong to the same stamp of
imagination that created Olympian and itan, sir and tun. We must go
far to the back of civilized thought to find parallels to such a story
as that in which the British sungod, struck by a rival in love with a
poisoned spear, is turned into an eagle, from whose wound great pieces
of carrion are continually failing.

his aspect of the eltic literary records was clearly seen, and
eloquently expressed, by atthew Arnold in his tudy of eltic
iterature. e was referring to the Welsh side, but his image holds
good equally for the aelic. â€he first thing that strikes oneâ€, he
says, â€in reading the abinogion is how evidently the medival
storyteller is pillaging an antiquity of which he does not fully
possess the secret he is like a peasant building his hut on the site of
alicarnassus or phesus he builds, but what he builds is full of
materials of which he knows not the history, or knows by a glimmering
tradition merely stones â€not of this buildingâ€, but of an older
architecture, greater, cunninger, more maestical.â€ is heroes â€are no
medival personages they belong to an older, pagan, mythological
worldâ€. o, too, with the figures, however euhemerized, of the three
great aelic cycles that of the uatha  anann, of the eroes of
lster, of inn and the enians. heir divinity outshines their
humanity through their masks may be seen the faces of gods.

et, gods as they are, they had taken on the semblance of mortality by
the time their histories were fixed in the form in which we have them
now. heir earliest records, if those could be restored to us, would
doubtless show them eternal and undying, changing their shapes at will,
but not passing away. But the posthristian copyists, whether Irish or
Welsh, would not countenance this. ence we have the singular paradox of
the deaths of Immortals. here is hardly one of the figures of either
the aelic or the British antheon whose demise is not somewhere
recorded. sually they fell in the unceasing battles between the
divinities of darkness and of light. heir deaths in earlier cycles of
myth, however, do not preclude their appearance in later ones. Only,
indeed, with the closing of the lips of the last mortal who preserved
his tradition can the life of a god be truly said to end.

But, before proceeding to recount the myths of the â€Ancient Britonsâ€, it
will be well to decide what people, exactly, we mean by that loose but
convenient phrase. We have, all of us, vague ideas of Ancient Britons,
recollected, doubtless, from our schoolbooks. here we saw their
pictures as, painted with woad, they paddled coracles, or drove scythed
chariots through legions of astonished omans. heir ruids,
whitebearded and wearing long, white robes, cut the mistletoe with a
golden sickle at the time of the full moon, or, less innocently
employed, made bonfires of human beings shut up in gigantic figures of
wickerwork.

uch picturesque details were little short of the sumtotal, not only of
our own knowledge of the subect, but also of that of our teachers.
ractically all their information concerning the ancient inhabitants of
Britain was taken from the ommentaries of ulius aesar. o far as it
went, it was no doubt correct but it did not go far. aesarâ€s interest
in our British ancestors was that of a general who was his own
warcorrespondent rather than that of an exhaustive and painstaking
scientist. It has been reserved for modern archologists, philologists,
and ethnologists to give us a fuller account of the Ancient Britons.

he inhabitants of our islands previous to the oman invasion are
generally described as â€eltsâ€. But they must have been largely a mixed
race and the people with whom they mingled must have modified to
someâ€and perhaps to a largeâ€extent their physique, their customs, and
their language.

peculation has run somewhat wild over the question of the composition
of the arly Britons. But out of the clash of rival theories there
emerges oneâ€and one onlyâ€which may be considered as scientifically
established. We have certain proof of two distinct human stocks in the
British Islands at the time of the oman onquest and so great an
authority as rofessor uxley has given his opinion that there is no
evidence of any others.

he earliest of these two races would seem to have inhabited our islands
from the most ancient times, and may, for our purpose, be described as
aboriginal. It was the people that built the â€long barrowsâ€ and which
is variously called by ethnologists the Iberian, editerranean, Berber,
Basque, ilurian, or uskarian race. In physique it was short, swarthy,
darkhaired, darkeyed, and longskulled its language belonged to the
class called â€amiticâ€, the surviving types of which are found among the
allas, Abyssinians, Berbers, and other orth African tribes and it
seems to have come originally from some part either of astern,
orthern, or entral Africa. preading thence, it was probably the first
people to inhabit the alley of the ile, and it sent offshoots into
yria and Asia inor. he earliest ellenes found it in reece under the
name of â€elasgoiâ€ the earliest atins in Italy, as the â€truscansâ€
and the ebrews in alestine, as the â€ittitesâ€. It spread northward
through urope as far as the Baltic, and westward, along the Atlas
chain, to pain, rance, and our own islands. In many countries it
reached a comparatively high level of civilization, but in Britain its
development must have been early checked. We can discern it as an
agricultural rather than a pastoral people, still in the tone Age,
dwelling in totemistic tribes on hills whose summits it fortified
elaborately, and whose slopes it cultivated on what is called the
â€terrace systemâ€, and having a primitive culture which ethnologists
think to have much resembled that of the present hilltribes of outhern
India. It held our islands till the coming of the elts, who fought
with the aborigines, dispossessed them of the more fertile parts,
subugated them, even amalgamated with them, but certainly never
extirpated them. In the time of the omans they were still practically
independent in outh Wales. In Ireland they were long unconquered, and
are found as allies rather than serfs of the aels, ruling their own
provinces, and preserving their own customs and religion. or, in spite
of all the successive invasions of reat Britain and Ireland, are they
yet extinct, or so merged as to have lost their type, which is still the
predominant one in many parts of the west both of Britain and Ireland,
and is believed by some ethnologists to be generally upon the increase
all over ngland.

he second of the two races was the exact opposite to the first. It was
the tall, fair, lighthaired, blue or grayeyed, broadheaded people
called, popularly, the â€eltsâ€, who belonged in speech to the â€Aryanâ€
family, their language finding its affinities in atin, reek, eutonic,
lavic, the end of Ancient ersia, and the anscrit of Ancient India.
Its original home was probably somewhere in entral urope, along the
course of the upper anube, or in the region of the Alps. he â€round
barrowsâ€ in which it buried its dead, or deposited their burnt ashes,
differ in shape from the â€long barrowsâ€ of the earlier race. It was in a
higher stage of culture than the â€Iberiansâ€, and introduced into Britain
bronze and silver, and, perhaps, some of the more lately domesticated
animals.

Both Iberians and elts were divided into numerous tribes, but there is
nothing to show that there was any great diversity among the former. It
is otherwise with the elts, who were separated into two main branches
which came over at different times. he earliest were the oidels, or
aels the second, the Brythons, or Britons. Between these two branches
there was not only a dialectical, but probably, also, a considerable
physical difference. ome anthropologists even postulate a different
shape of skull. Without necessarily admitting this, there is reason to
suppose a difference of build and of colour of hair. With regard to
this, we have the evidence of atin writersâ€of acitus, who tells us
that the â€aledoniansâ€ of the orth differed from the outhern Britons
in being largerlimbed and redderhaired, and of trabo, who
described the tribes in the interior of Britain as taller than the
aulish colonists on the coast, with hair less yellow and limbs more
loosely knit. qually do the classic authorities agree in recognizing
the â€iluresâ€ of outh Wales as an entirely different race from any
other in Britain. he dark complexions and curly hair of these Iberians
seemed to acitus to prove them immigrants from pain.

rofessor hys also puts forward evidence to show that the oidels and
the Brythons had already separated before they first left aul for our
islands. e finds them as two distinct peoples there. We do not
expect so much nowadays from â€the merest schoolboyâ€ as we did in
acaulayâ€s time, but even the modern descendant of that paragon could
probably tell us that all aul was divided into three parts, one of
which was inhabited by the Belgae, another by the Aquitani, and the
third by those who called themselves eltae, but were termed alli by
the omans and that they all differed from one another in language,
customs, and laws. Of these, rofessor hys identifies the Belgae
with the Brythons, and the eltae with the oidels, the third people,
the Aquitani, being noneltic and nonAryan, part of the great
amiticspeaking Iberian stock. he eltae, with their oidelic
dialect of eltic, which survives today in the aelic languages of
Ireland, cotland, and the Isle of an, were the first to come over to
Britain, pushed forward, probably, by the Belgae, who, aesar tells us,
were the bravest of the auls. ere they conquered the native
Iberians, driving them out of the fertile parts into the rugged
districts of the north and west. ater came the Belgae themselves,
compelled by press of population and they, bringing better weapons and
a higher civilization, treated the oidels as those had treated the
Iberians. hus harried, the oidels probably combined with the Iberians
against what was now the common foe, and became to a large degree
amalgamated with them. he result was that during the oman domination
the British Islands were roughly divided with regard to race as follows
he Brythons, or second eltic race, held all Britain south of the
weed, with the exception of the extreme west, while the first eltic
race, the oidelic, had most of Ireland, as well as the Isle of an,
umberland, the West ighlands, ornwall, evon, and orth Wales. orth
of the rampians lived the icts, who were probably more or less
oidelicized Iberians, the aboriginal race also holding out, unmixed, in
outh Wales and parts of Ireland.

It is now time to decide what, for the purposes of this book, it will be
best to call the two different branches of the elts, and their
languages. With such familiar terms as â€aelâ€ and â€Britonâ€, â€aelicâ€ and
â€Britishâ€, ready to our hands, it seems pedantic to insist upon the more
technical â€oidelâ€ and â€Brythonâ€, â€oidelicâ€ and â€Brythonicâ€. he
difficulty is that the words â€aelâ€ and â€aelicâ€ have been so long
popularly used to designate only the modern â€oidelsâ€ of cotland and
their language, that they may create confusion when also applied to the
people and languages of Ireland and the Isle of an. imilarly, the
words â€Britonâ€ and â€Britishâ€ have come to mean, at the present day, the
people of the whole of the British Islands, though they at first only
signified the inhabitants of ngland, entral Wales, the owlands of
cotland, and the Brythonic colony in Brittany. owever, the words
â€oidelâ€ and â€Brythonâ€, with their derivatives, are so clumsy that it
will probably prove best to use the neater terms. In this volume,
therefore, the â€oidelsâ€ of Ireland, cotland, and the Isle of an are
our â€aelsâ€ and the â€Brythonsâ€ of ngland and Wales are our â€Britonsâ€.

We get the earliest accounts of the life of the inhabitants of the
British Islands from two sources. he first is a foreign one, that of
the atin writers. But the omans only really knew the outhern Britons,
whom they describe as similar in physique and customs to the ontinental
auls, with whom, indeed, they considered them to be identical. At
the time they wrote, colonies of Belgae were still settling upon the
coasts of Britain opposite to aul. oman information grew scantier
as it approached the Wall, and of the orthern tribes they seem to have
had only such knowledge as they gathered through occasional warfare with
them. hey describe them as entirely barbarous, naked and tattooed,
living by the chase alone, without towns, houses, or fields, without
government or family life, and regarding iron as an ornament of value,
as other, more civilized peoples regarded gold. As for Ireland, it
never came under their direct observation, and we are entirely dependent
upon its native writers for information as to the manners and customs of
the aels. It may be considered convincing proof of the authenticity of
the descriptions of life contained in the ancient aelic manuscripts
that they corroborate so completely the observations of the atin
writers upon the Britons and auls. eading the two side by side, we may
largely reconstruct the common civilization of the elts.

oughly speaking, one may compare it with the civilization of the
reeks, as described by omer. Both peoples were in the tribal and
pastoral stage of culture, in which the chiefs are the great
cattleowners round whom their less wealthy fellows gather. Both wear
much the same attire, use the same kind of weapons, and fight in the
same mannerâ€from the warchariot, a vehicle already obsolete even in
Ireland by the first century of the hristian era. Battles are fought
singlehanded between chiefs, the illarmed common people contributing
little to their result, and less to their history. uch chiefs are said
to be divinely descendedâ€sons, even, of the immortal gods. heir
tremendous feats are sung by the bards, who, like the omeric poets,
were privileged persons, inferior only to the warlord. Ancient reek
and Ancient elt had very much the same conceptions of life, both as
regards this world and the next.

We may gather much detailed information of the early inhabitants of the
British Islands from our various authorities. heir clothes, which
consisted, according to the atin writers, of a blouse with sleeves,
trousers fitting closely round the ankles, and a shawl or cloak,
fastened at the shoulder with a brooch, were made either of thick felt
or of woven cloth dyed with various brilliant colours. he writer
iodorus tells us that they were crossed with little squares and lines,
â€as though they had been sprinkled with flowersâ€. hey were, in fact,
like â€tartansâ€, and we may believe arro, who tells us that they â€made a
gaudy showâ€. he men alone seem to have worn hats, which were of soft
felt, the womenâ€s hair being uncovered, and tied in a knot behind. In
time of battle, the men also dispensed with any headcovering, brushing
their abundant hair forward into a thick mass, and dyeing it red with a
soap made of goatâ€s fat and beech ashes, until they looked (says
iceroâ€s tutor osidonius, who visited Britain about  B..) less like
human beings than wild men of the woods. Both sexes were fond of
ornaments, which took the form of gold bracelets, rings, pins, and
brooches, and of beads of amber, glass, and et. heir knives, daggers,
spearheads, axes, and swords were made of bronze or iron their shields
were the same round target used by the ighlanders at the battle of
ulloden and they seem also to have had a kind of lasso to which a
hammershaped ball was attached, and which they used as the auchos of
outh America use their bola. heir warchariots were made of wicker,
the wooden wheels being armed with sickles of bronze. hese were drawn
either by two or four horses, and were large enough to hold several
persons in each. tanding in these, they rushed along the enemyâ€s lines,
hurling darts, and driving the scythes against all who came within
reach. he omans were much impressed by the skill of the drivers, who
â€could check their horses at full speed on a steep incline, and turn
them in an instant, and could run along the pole, and stand on the yoke,
and then get back into their chariots again without a momentâ€s
delayâ€.

With these accounts of the oman writers we may compare the picture of
the aelic hero, uchulainn, as the ancient Irish writers describe him
dressed and armed for battle. lorified by the bard, he yet wears
essentially the same costume and equipment which the classic historians
and geographers described more soberly. â€is gorgeous raiment that he
wore in great conventionsâ€ consisted of â€a fair crimson tunic of five
plies and fringed, with a long pin of white silver, goldenchased and
patterned, shining as if it had been a luminous torch which for its
blazing property and brilliance men might not endure to see. ext his
skin, a bodyvest of silk, bordered and fringed all round with gold,
with silver, and with white bronze, which vest came as far as the upper
edge of his russetcoloured kilt.... About his neck were a hundred
linklets of red gold that flashed again, with pendants hanging from
them. is headgear was adorned with a hundred mixed carbuncle ewels,
strung.â€ e carried â€a trusty special shield, in hue dark crimson, and
in its circumference armed with a pure white silver rim. At his left
side a long and goldenhilted sword. Beside him, in the chariot, a
lengthy spear together with a keen, aggressionboding avelin, fitted
with hurling thong, with rivets of white bronze.â€ Another passage of

wickerwork, moving on wheels of white bronze. It had a high rounded
frame of creaking copper, a strong curved yoke of gold, and a pole of
white silver, with mountings of white bronze. he yellow reins were
plaited, and the shafts were as hard and straight as swordblades.

In like manner the ancient Irish writers have made glorious the halls
and fortresses of their mythical kings. ike the palaces of riam, of
enelaus, and of Odysseus, they gleam with gold and gems. onchobar,
the legendary ing of lster in its golden age, had three such â€housesâ€
at main acha. Of the one called the â€ed Branchâ€, we are told that it
contained nine compartments of red yew, partitioned by walls of bronze,
all grouped around the kingâ€s private chamber, which had a ceiling of
silver, and bronze pillars adorned with gold and carbuncles. But the
far less magnificent accounts of the atin writers have, no doubt, more
truth in them than such lavish pictures. hey described the Britons they
knew as living in villages of beehive huts, roofed with fern or thatch,
from which, at the approach of an enemy, they retired to the local
dn. his, so far from being elaborate, merely consisted of a round or
oval space fenced in with palisades and earthworks, and situated either
upon the top of a hill or in the midst of a not easily traversable
morass. We may see the remains of such strongholds in many parts of
nglandâ€notable ones are the â€castlesâ€ of Amesbury, Avebury, and Old
arum in Wiltshire, aint atherineâ€s ill, near Winchester, and aint
eorgeâ€s ill, in urreyâ€and it is probable that, in spite of the eltic
praisers of past days, the â€palacesâ€ of main acha and of ara were
very like them.

he eltic customs were, like the omeric, those of the primitive world.
All land (though it may have theoretically belonged to the chief) was
cultivated in common. his community of possessions is stated by
aesar to have extended to their wives but the imputation cannot be
said to have been proved. On the contrary, in the stories of both
branches of the eltic race, women seem to have taken a higher place in
menâ€s estimation, and to have enoyed far more personal liberty, than
among the omeric reeks. he idea may have arisen from a
misunderstanding of some of the curious eltic customs. escent seems to
have been traced through the maternal rather than through the paternal
line, a very unAryan procedure which some believe to have been borrowed
from another race. he parental relation was still further lessened by
the custom of sending children to be brought up outside the family in
which they were born, so that they had fosterparents to whom they were
as much, or even more, attached than to their natural ones.

heir political state, mirroring their family life, was not less
primitive. here was no central tribunal. isputes were settled within
the families in which they occurred, while, in the case of graver
inuries, the inured party or his nearest relation could kill the
culprit or exact a fine from him. As families increased in number, they
became petty tribes, often at war with one another. A defeated tribe had
to recognize the sovereignty of the head man of the conquering tribe,
and a succession of such victories exalted him into the position of a
chief of his district. But even then, though his decision was the whole
of the law, he was little more than the mouthpiece of public opinion.

he ancient inhabitants of Britainâ€the aelic and British eltsâ€have
been already described as forming a branch of what are roughly called
the â€Aryansâ€. his name has, however, little reference to race, and
really signifies the speakers of a group of languages which can be all
shown to be connected, and to descend remotely from a single sourceâ€a
hypothetical mothertongue spoken by a hypothetical people which we term
â€Aryanâ€, or, more correctly, â€Indouropeanâ€. his primeval speech,
evolved, probably, upon some part of the great plain which stretches
from the mountains of entral urope to the mountains of entral Asia,
has spread, superseding, or amalgamating with the tongues of other
races, until branches of it are spoken over almost the whole of urope
and a great portion of Asia. All the various atin, reek, lavic,
eutonic, and eltic languages are â€Aryanâ€, as well as ersian and other
Asiatic dialects derived from the ancient â€endâ€, and the numerous
Indian languages which trace their origin to anscrit.

ot very long ago, it was supposed that this common descent of language
involved a common descent of blood. A real brotherhood was
enthusiastically claimed for all the principal uropean nations, who
were also invited to recognize indus and ersians as their longlost
cousins. ince then, it has been conceded that, while the Aryan speech
survived, though greatly modified, the Aryan blood might well have
disappeared, diluted beyond recognition by crossing with the other races
whom the Aryans conquered, or among whom they more or less peacefully
settled. As a matter of fact, there are no uropean nationsâ€perhaps no
people at all except a few remote savage tribesâ€which are not made up of
the most diverse elements. Aryan and nonAryan long ago blended
inextricably, to form by their fusion new peoples.

But, ust as the Aryan speech influenced the new languages, and the
Aryan customs the new civilizations, so we can still discern in the
religions of the Aryanspeaking nations similar ideas and expressions
pointing to an original source of mythological conceptions. ence,
whether we investigate the mythology of the indus, the reeks, the
eutons, or the elts, we find the same mythological groundwork. In
each, we see the powers of nature personified, and endowed with human
form and attributes, though bearing, with few exceptions, different
names. ike the edic brahmans, the reek and atin poets, and the orse
scalds, the eltic bardsâ€whether aels or Britonsâ€imagined the sky, the
sun, the moon, the earth, the sea, and the dark underworld, as well as
the mountains, the streams and the woods, to be ruled by beings like
their own chiefs, but infinitely more powerful every passion, as War
and ove, and every art, as oetry and mithcraft, had its divine
founder, teacher, and exponent and of all these deities and their
imagined children, they wove the poetical and allegorical romances which
form the subect of the present volume.

ike other nations, too, whether Aryan or nonAryan, the elts had,
besides their mythology, a religion. It is not enough to tell tales of
shadowy gods they must be made visible by sculpture, housed in groves
or temples, served with ritual, and propitiated with sacrifices, if one
is to hope for their favours. very cult must have its priests living by
the altar.

he priests of the elts are wellknown to us by name as the â€ruidsâ€â€a
word derived from a root  which signifies a tree, and especially the
oak, in several Aryan languages. his is generallyâ€though not by all
scholarsâ€taken as proving that they paid an especial veneration to the
king of trees. It is true that the mistletoeâ€that strange parasite upon
the oakâ€was prominent among their â€herbs of powerâ€, and played a part in
their ritual but this is equally true of other Aryan nations. By
the orse it was held sacred to the god Balder, while the omans
believed it to be the â€golden boughâ€ that gave access to ades.

he accounts both of the atin and aelic writers give us a fairly
complete idea of the nature of the ruids, and especially of the high
estimation in which they were held. hey were at once the priests, the
physicians, the wizards, the diviners, the theologians, the scientists,
and the historians of their tribes. All spiritual power and all human
knowledge were vested in them, and they ranked second only to the kings
and chiefs. hey were freed from all contribution to the tate, whether
by tribute or service in war, so that they might the better apply
themselves to their divine offices. heir decisions were absolutely
final, and those who disobeyed them were laid under a terrible
excommunication or â€boycottâ€. lassic writers tell us how they
lorded it in aul, where, no doubt, they borrowed splendour by imitating
their more civilized neighbours. en of the highest rank were proud to
cast aside the insignia of mere mortal honour to oin the company of
those who claimed to be the direct mediators with the skygod and the
thundergod, and who must have resembled the ecclesiastics of medival
urope in the days of their greatest power, combining, like them,
spiritual and temporal dignities, and possessing the highest culture of
their age. et it was not among these ruids of aul, with their
splendid temples and vestments and their elaborate rituals, that the
metropolis of ruidism was to be sought. We learn from aesar that the
allic ruids believed their religion to have come to them, originally,
from Britain, and that it was their practice to send their â€theological
studentsâ€ across the hannel to learn its doctrines at their purest
source. o trace a cult backwards is often to take a retrograde
course in culture, and it was no doubt in Britainâ€which liny the lder
tells us â€might have taught magic to ersiaâ€â€that the sufficiently
primitive and savage rites of the ruids of aul were preserved in their
still more savage and primitive forms. It is curious corroboration of
this alleged British origin of ruidism that the ancient Irish also
believed their ruidism to have come from the sister island. heir
heroes and seers are described as only gaining the highest knowledge by
travelling to Alba. owever this may be, we may take it as certain
that this ruidism was the accepted religion of the eltic race.

ertain scholars look deeper for its origin, holding its dark
superstitions and savage rites to bear the stamp of lower minds than
those of the poetic and manly elts. rofessor hys inclines to see
three forms of religion in the British Islands at the time of the oman
invasion the â€ruidismâ€ of the Iberian aborigines the pure polytheism
of the Brythons, who, having come later into the country, had mixed but
little with the natives and the mingled Aryan and nonAryan cults of
the oidels, who were already largely amalgamated with them. But
many authorities dissent from this view, and, indeed, we are not obliged
to postulate borrowing from tribes in a lower state of culture, to
explain primitive and savage features underlying a higher religion. he
â€Aryanâ€ nations must have passed, equally with all others, through a

Of the famous teaching of the ruids we know little, owing to their
habit of never allowing their doctrines to be put into writing. aesar,

concerning the heavenly bodies and their movements, the size of the

Another legend tells how the famous inn mac oul was reborn, after two

uch ideas, however, belonged to the metaphysical side of ruidism. ar
more important to the practical primitive mind are ritual and sacrifice,
by the due performance of which the gods are persuaded or compelled to

humouring of the divinities took the shape of human sacrifice, and that
upon a scale which would seem to have been unsurpassed in horror even by
the most savage tribes of West Africa or olynesia. â€he whole aulish
nationâ€, says aesar, â€is to a great degree devoted to superstitious

the ruids as ministers for such sacrifices, because they think that,
unless the life of man be repaid for the life of man, the will of the
immortal gods cannot be appeased. hey also ordain national offerings of
the same kind. Others make wickerwork images of vast size, the limbs of
which they fill with living men and set on fire.â€

We find evidence of similarly awful customs in pagan Ireland. Among the

uch, we gather from a tradition which we may deem authentic, was human

It would appear probable that the eltic tate worship was what is
called â€solarâ€. All its chief festivals related to points in the sunâ€s

It was doubtless at tonehenge that the British Apollo was thus seen

he same great gods were, no doubt, adored by all the elts, not only of

they can be traced by name right across the ancient eltic world. In
other cases, what is obviously the same personified power of nature is

worshipped by certain tribes alone, to whom they stood as ancestors and

could exist side by side, both even claiming the same votary. It needs
the stern faith of monotheism to deny the existence of the gods of
others. olytheistic nations have seldom or never risen to such a
height. In their dealings with a conquered people, the conquerors
naturally held their own gods to be the stronger. till, it could not be
denied that the gods of the conquered were upon their own ground they
knew, so to speak, the country, and might have unguessed powers of doing
evil What if, to avenge their worshippers and themselves, they were to
make the land barren and useless to the conquerors o that conquering
pagan nations have usually been quite ready to stretch out the hand of
welcome to the deities of their new subects, to propitiate them by
sacrifice, and even to admit them within the pale of their own antheon.

his raises the question of the exact nationality of the gods whose
stories we are about to tell. Were they all Aryan, or did any of the
greater aboriginal deities climb up to take their place among the aelic
tribe of the goddess anu, or the British children of the goddess n

legends make up â€he ythology of the British Islandsâ€.

Of the two eltic races that settled in our islands, it is the earlier,
the aels, that has best preserved its old mythology. It is true that we
have in few cases such detailed account of the aelic gods as we gain of
the ellenic deities from the reek poets, of the Indian evas from the
ig eda, or of the orse sir from the ddas. et none the less may we
draw from the ancient Irish manuscripts quite enough information to
enable us to set forth their figures with some clearness. We find them,
as might have been anticipated, very much like the divine hierarchies of
other Aryan peoples.

We also find them separated into two opposing camps, a division common
to all the Aryan religions. ust as the Olympians struggled with the
iants, the sir fought the tuns, and the evas the Asuras, so there
is warfare in the aelic spiritual world between two superhuman hosts.
On one side are ranged the gods of day, light, life, fertility, wisdom,
and good on the other, the demons of night, darkness, death,
barrenness, and evil. he first were the great spirits symbolizing the
beneficial aspects of nature and the arts and intelligence of man the
second were the hostile powers thought to be behind such baneful
manifestations as storm and fog, drought and disease. he first are
ranged as a divine family round a goddess called anu, from whom they
took their wellknown name of uatha  anann, â€ribeâ€ or â€olk
of the oddess anuâ€. he second owned allegiance to a female divinity
called omnu their king, Indech, is described as her son, and they are
all called â€omnuâ€s godsâ€. he word â€omnuâ€ appears to have signified
the abyss or the deep sea, and the same idea is also expressed in
their betterknown name of â€omorsâ€, derived from two aelic words
meaning â€under seaâ€. he waste of water seems to have always
impressed the elts with the sense of primeval ancientness it was
connected in their minds with vastness, darkness, and monstrous
birthsâ€the very antithesis of all that was symbolized by the earth, the
sky, and the sun.

herefore the omors were held to be more ancient than the gods, before
whom they were, however, destined to fall in the end. Offspring of
â€haos and Old ightâ€, they were, for the most part, huge and deformed.
ome had but one arm and one leg apiece, while others had the heads of
goats, horses, or bulls. he most famous, and perhaps the most
terrible of them all was Balor, whose father is said to have been one
Buarainech, that is, the â€cowfacedâ€, and who combined in himself
the two classical rles of the yclops and the edusa. hough he had two
eyes, one was always kept shut, for it was so venomous that it slew
anyone on whom its look fell. his malignant quality of Balorâ€s eye was
not natural to him, but was the result of an accident. rged by
curiosity, he once looked in at the window of a house where his fatherâ€s
sorcerers were preparing a magic potion, and the poisonous smoke from
the cauldron reached his eye, infecting it with so much of its own
deadly nature as to make it disastrous to others. either god nor giant
seems to have been exempt from its dangers so that Balor was only
allowed to live on condition that he kept his terrible eye shut. On days
of battle he was placed opposite to the enemy, the lid of the destroying
eye was lifted up with a hook, and its gaze withered all who stood
before it. he memory of Balor and his eye still lingers in Ireland the
â€eye of Balorâ€ is the name for what the peasantry of other countries
call the â€evil eyeâ€ stories are still told of Balar Beimann, or
â€Balor of the ighty Blowsâ€ and â€Balorâ€s astleâ€ is the name of a
curious cliff on ory Island. his island, off the coast of onegal, was
the omorian outpost upon earth, their real abode being in the cold
depths of the sea.

his rule, however, as to the hideousness of the omors had its
exceptions. lathan, one of their chiefs, is described in an old

Balor, Bress, and lathan are the three omorian personages whose

they are only a few out of many, nor are they the oldest. We can learn,
however, nothing but a few names of any ancestors of the aelic giants.
his is equally true of the aelic gods. hose we know are evidently not

After uada, the greatest of the gods was the agda, whose name seems to

he origin of this alphabet is obscure. ome authorities consider it of

seems, at any rate, to have been based upon, and consequently to

Ogma, besides being the patron of literature, was the champion, or

he people of the goddess anu were not the first divine inhabitants of
Ireland. Others had been before them, dwellers in â€the dark backward and
abysm of timeâ€. In this the eltic mythology resembles those of other
nations, in almost all of which we find an old, dim realm of gods
standing behind the reigning antheon. uch were ronos and the itans,
dispossessed by the eus who seemed, even to esiod, something of a
parvenu deity. aelic tradition recognizes two divine dynasties
anterior to the uatha  anann. he first of these was called â€he
ace of artholonâ€. Its head and leader cameâ€as all gods and men came,
according to eltic ideasâ€from the Other World, and landed in Ireland
with a retinue of twentyfour males and twentyfour females upon the
first of ay, the day called â€Beltaineâ€, sacred to Bil, the god of
death. At this remote time, Ireland consisted of only one treeless,
grassless plain, watered by three lakes and nine rivers. But, as the
race of artholon increased, the land stretched, or widened, under
themâ€some said miraculously, and others, by the labours of artholonâ€s
people. At any rate, during the three hundred years they dwelt there, it
grew from one plain to four, and acquired seven new lakes which was
fortunate, for the race of artholon increased from fortyeight members
to five thousand, in spite of battles with the omors.

hese would seem to have been inevitable. Whatever gods ruled, they
found themselves in eternal opposition to the notgodsâ€the powers of
darkness, winter, evil, and death. he race of artholon warred against
them with success. At the lain of Ith, artholon defeated their leader,
a gigantic demon called ichol the ootless, and dispersed his deformed
and monstrous host. After this there was quiet for three hundred years.
henâ€upon the same fatal first of ayâ€there began a mysterious epidemic,
which lasted a week, and destroyed them all. In premonition of their
end, they foregathered upon the original, firstcreated plainâ€then
called en ag, or the â€Old lainâ€,â€so that those who survived might
the more easily bury those that died. heir funeralplace is still
marked by a mound near ublin, called â€allaghtâ€ in the maps, but
formerly known as amlecht uintre artholain, the â€laguegrave of
artholonâ€s eopleâ€. his would seem to have been a development of the
very oldest form of the legendâ€which knew nothing of a plague, but
merely represented the people of artholon as having returned, after
their soourn in Ireland, to the other world, whence they cameâ€and is
probably due to the gradual euhemerization of the ancient gods into
ancient men.

ollowing the race of artholon, came the race of emed, which carried
on the work and traditions of its forerunner. uring its time, Ireland
again enlarged herself, to the extent of twelve new plains and four more
lakes. ike the people of artholon, the race of emed struggled with
the omors, and defeated them in four consecutive battles. hen emed
died, with two thousand of his people, from an epidemic, and the
remnant, left without their leader, were terribly oppressed by the
omors. wo omorian kingsâ€orc, son of ela, and onann, son of
ebarâ€had built a tower of glass upon ory Island, always their chief
stronghold, and where stories of them still linger, and from this
vantagepoint they dictated a tax which recalls that paid, in reek
story, to the retan inotaur. wothirds of the children born to the
race of emed during the year were to be delivered up on each day of
amhain. oaded by this to a last desperate effort, the survivors of
emedâ€s people attacked the tower, and took it, onann perishing in the
struggle. But their triumph was short. orc, the other king, collected
his forces, and inflicted such a slaughter upon the people of emed
that, out of the sixteen thousand who had assembled for the storming of
the tower, only thirty survived. And these returned whence they came, or
diedâ€the two acts being, mythologically speaking, the same.

One cannot help seeing a good deal of similarity between the stories of
these two mythical invasions of Ireland. specially noticeable is the
account of the epidemic which destroyed all artholonâ€s people and
nearly all of emedâ€s. ence it has been held that the two legends are
duplicates, and that there was at first only one, which has been adapted
somewhat differently by two races, the Iberians and the aels. rofessor
hys considers the account of emed to have been the original eltic
one, and the artholon story, the version of it which the native races
made to please themselves. he name â€artholonâ€, with its initial p,
is entirely foreign to the genius of aelic speech. oreover, artholon
himself is given, by the early chroniclers, ancestors whose decidedly
nonAryan names reappear afterwards as the names of ir Bolg chiefs.
emed was later than artholon in Ireland, as the aels, or â€ilesiansâ€,
were later than the Iberians, or â€ir Bolgsâ€.

hese â€ir Bolgsâ€ are found in myth as the next colonizers of Ireland.
arying traditions say that they came from reece, or from â€painâ€â€which

aillionâ€ or â€en of aillionâ€, and the â€ir Bolgâ€ or â€en of Bolgâ€

stories are told of their life in reece, and how they came to Ireland

earliest tradition.

In the time of their domination they had, we are told, partitioned

was ochaid son of rc, surnamed â€he roudâ€.

We have practically no other details regarding their life in Ireland. It
is obvious, however, that they were not really gods, but the preAryan
race which the aels, when they landed in Ireland, found already in
occupation. here are many instances of peoples at a certain stage of
culture regarding tribes in a somewhat lower one as semidivine, or,
rather, halfdiabolical. he suspicion and fear with which the early

than humanâ€. hey feared them for the weird magical rites which they

he enemies alike of ir Bolg and omor, the uatha  anann, gods of

Whether the uatha  anann came from earth or heaven, they landed in a

â€druidicallyformed showers and fogsustaining showercloudsâ€ over the

he uatha  anann, advancing westward, had reached a place called the
â€lain of the eaâ€, in einster, when the two armies met. ach sent out

he ir Bolg envoy advised his people to accept this offer. But their

he ir Bolgs followed them, and encamped on the nearer side of the

â€hen whenâ€, asked the envoy, â€do you intend to give battleâ€

â€We must have a truce,â€ they said, â€for we want time to repair our

he result of this chivalrous, but, to modern ideas, amazing, parley was
that a truce of one hundred and five days was agreed upon.

It was on idsummer ay that the opposing armies at last met. he people

he whole story has a singularly historical, curiously unmythological

cairns of stones, too, are scattered over what was certainly once the
scene of a great battle. hese various prehistoric monuments each
have their stilltold story and ir William Wilde, as he relates in his
ough orrib, was so impressed by the unexpected agreement
between the details of the legendary battle, as he read them in the
ancient manuscript, and the traditions still attaching to the mounds,
circles, and cairns, that he tells us he could not help coming to the
conclusion that the account was absolutely historical. ertainly the
coincidences are curious. is opinion was that the â€ir Bolgsâ€ were a
colony of Belg, and that the â€uatha  anannâ€ were anes. But the
people of the goddess anu are too obviously mythical to make it worth
while to seek any standingground for them in the world of reality. In
their superhuman attributes, they are quite different from the ir
Bolgs. In the epical cycle it is made as clear that the uatha  anann
are divine beings as it is that the ir Bolg, the ir omnann, and the
ir aillion stand on exactly the same footing as the men of lster.
ater history records by what ilesian kings and on what terms of
rackrent the three tribes were allowed settlements in other parts of
Ireland than their native onnaught. hey appear in ancient, medival,
and almost modern chronicles as the old race of the land. he truth
seems to be that the whole story of the war between the gods and the ir
Bolgs is an invention of comparatively late times. In the earliest
documents there is only one battle of oytura, fought between the people
of the goddess anu and the omors. he idea of doubling it seems to
date from after the eleventh century and its inventor may very
well have used the legends concerning this battlefield, where two
unknown armies had fought in days gone by, in compiling his story. It
never belonged to the same genuine mythological stratum as the legend of
the original battle fought by the uatha  anann, the gods of the
aels, against the omors, the gods of the Iberians.

It was as a result of the loss of his hand in this battle with the ir

hey agreed that it would be a politic thing for them to conciliate the

But, in spite of all his fair promises, Bress, who belonged in heart to

taxes. e put a tax upon every hearth, upon every kneadingtrough, and

cattle in Ireland between two fires, so that their hair was singed off,
and thus obtained the monopoly of the main source of food. o earn a
livelihood, all the gods, even the greatest, were now forced to labour
for him. Ogma, their champion, was sent out to collect firewood, while
the agda was put to work building forts and castles.

One day, when the agda was at his task, his son, Angus, came to him.
â€ou have nearly finished that castle,â€ he said. â€What reward do you

he agda finished building the fort, and then went to Bress for his

Angus had told him of. he king, who had expected to be asked very much

eanwhile Bress was infuriating the people of the goddess anu by adding

It was at this crisis that two physicians, iach, the son, and Airmid,
the daughter, of iancecht, the god of medicine, came to the castle

â€perhaps you can give me a new eye.â€ â€ertainly,â€ they said, â€we could

he story goes on to say that this was not wholly a benefit to him for

When iancecht, iachâ€s father, heard of this, he was very angry to

And upon his grave there grew up three hundred and sixtyfive stalks of

his tale is evidently the work of some ancient Irish storyteller who

By this time the seven years of preparation had come to an end. A week
before the ay of amhain, the orrg discovered that the omors had
landed upon rin. he at once sent a messenger to tell the agda, who
ordered his druids and sorcerers to go to the ford of the iver nius,
in ligo, and utter incantations against them.

he people of the goddess anu, however, were not yet quite ready for

he was of such food. hey poured into their kingâ€s cauldron, which was

we shall put you to death, for we will not have you go back to your own
people and say that the omors are inhospitable.â€ But they did not

that two persons of our puny size might have reclined comfortably in the
middle of it, dipped it into the porridge, and fished up halves of
salted pork and quarters of bacon.

house, and stood out like a sail before the wind.

But the omorsâ€ little practical oke upon the agda had given the

he war of gods and giants naturally mirrored the warfare of the aels,
in whose battles, as in those of most semibarbarous people, single
combat figured largely. he main armies stood still, while, every day,
duels took place between ambitious combatants. But no great warriors
either of the uatha  anann or of the omors took part in them.

ometimes a god, sometimes a giant would be the victor but there was a
difference in the net results that astonished the omors. If their own
swords and lances were broken, they were of no more use, and if their
own champions were killed, they never came back to life again but it
was quite otherwise with the people of the goddess anu. Weapons
shattered on one day reappeared upon the next in as good condition as
though they had never been used, and warriors slain on one day came back
upon the morrow unhurt, and ready, if necessary, to be killed again.

he omors decided to send someone to discover the secret of these
prodigies. he spy they chose was uadan, the son of Bress and of
Brigit, daughter of the agda, and therefore halfgiant and halfgod. e
disguised himself as a uatha  anann warrior, and went to look for
oibniu. e found him at his forge, together with uchtain, the
carpenter, and redn, the bronzeworker. e saw how oibniu forged
lanceheads with three blows of his hammer, while uchtain cut shafts
for them with three blows of his axe, and redn fixed the two parts
together so adroitly that his bronze nails needed no hammering in. e
went back and told the omors, who sent him again, this time to try and
kill oibniu.

e reappeared at the forge, and asked for a avelin. Without suspicion,
oibniu gave him one, and, as soon as he got it into his hand, he thrust
it through the smithâ€s body. But oibniu plucked it out, and, hurling it
back at his assailant, mortally wounded him. uadan went home to die,

Whenever one of the uatha  anann was killed or wounded, he was

water, and brought him back to life and health again.

he mystic spring was not long, however, allowed to help the people of

distinguish it from the other Battle of oytura fought by the uatha 
anann against the ir Bolgs farther to the south. ore of the omors
were killed in it, says the ancient manuscript, than there are stars in
the sky, grains of sand on the seashore, snowflakes in winter, drops

blades of grass trodden under horsesâ€ feet, or anannn son of râ€s

with the sole exception of arnac, in Brittany. egalithic
structures of almost every kind are found among themâ€stone cairns with
dolmens in their interiors, dolmens standing open and alone, dolmens

remains stand together upon an elevated plateau not more than a mile
across, and make the battlefield of oytura, though the least known,
perhaps the most impressive of all primeval ruins. What they really
commemorated we may never know, but, in all probability, the place was
the scene of some important and decisive early battle, the monuments
marking the graves of the chieftains who were interred as the result of
it. hose which have been examined were found to contain burnt wood and
the halfburnt bones of men and horses, as well as implements of flint
and bone. he actors, therefore, were still in the eolithic Age.
Whether the horses were domesticated ones buried with their riders, or
wild ones eaten at the funeral feasts, it would be hard to decide. he
history of the real event must have been long lost even at the early
date when its relics were pointed out as the records of a battle between
the gods and the giants of aelic myth.

he uatha  anann, following the routed omors, overtook and captured
