Of what Badb had in mind when she uttered this prophecy we have no
record. But it was true. he twilight of the Irish gods was at hand. A
new race was coming across the sea to dispute the ownership of Ireland
with the people of the goddess anu. And these newcomers were not
divinities like themselves, but men like ourselves, ancestors of the
aels.

his story of the conquest of the gods by mortalsâ€which seems such a
strange one to usâ€is typically eltic. he aelic mythology is the only
one which has preserved it in any detail but the doctrine would seem to
have been common at one time to all the elts. It was, however, of less
shame to the gods than would otherwise have been for men were of as
divine descent as themselves. he dogma of the elts was that men were
descended from the god of death, and first came from the and of the
ead to take possession of the present world. aesar tells us, in
his too short account of the auls, that they believed themselves to be
sprung from is ater, the god of the underworld. In the aelic
mythology is ater was called Bil, a name which has for root the
syllable bel, meaning â€to dieâ€. he god Beli in British mythology was
no doubt the same person, while the same idea is expressed by the same
root in the name of Balor, the terrible omor whose glance was
death.

he posthristian Irish chroniclers, seeking to reconcile hristian
teachings with the still vital pagan mythology by changing the gods into
ancient kings and incorporating them into the annals of the country,
with appropriate dates, also disposed of the genuine early doctrine by
substituting pain for ades, and giving a highlyfanciful account of
the origin and wanderings of their ancestors. o use a ibernicism,
appropriate in this connection, the first Irishman was a cythian called
enius arsa. eprived of his own throne, he had settled in gypt, where
his son iul married a daughter of the reigning haraoh. er name was
cta, and she had a son called oidel, whose greatgrandson was named
ber cot, the whole genealogy being probably invented to explain the
origin of the three names by which the aels called themselvesâ€inn,
cot, and oidel. enius and his family and clan were turned out of
gypt for refusing to oin in the persecution of the children of Israel,
and soourned in Africa for fortytwo years. heir wanderings took them
to â€the altars of the hilistines, by the ake of Osiersâ€ then, passing
between usicada and the hilly country of yria, they travelled through
auretania as far as the illars of ercules and thence landed in
pain, where they lived many years, greatly increasing and multiplying.
he same route is given by the twelfthcentury British historian,
eoffrey of onmouth, as that taken by Brutus and the roans when they
came to colonize Britain. Its only connection with any kind of fact
is that it corresponds fairly well with what ethnologists consider must
have been the westward line of migration taken, not, curiously enough,
by the Aryan elts, but by the preAryan Iberians.

It is sufficient for us to find the first men in pain, remembering that
â€painâ€ stood for the eltic ades, or lysium. In this country Bregon,
the father of two sons, Bil and Ith, had built a watchtower, from
which, one winterâ€s evening, Ith saw, far off over the seas, a land he
had never noticed before. â€It is on winter evenings, when the air is
pure, that manâ€s eyesight reaches farthestâ€, remarks the old tract
called the â€Book of Invasionsâ€, gravely accounting for the fact
that Ith saw Ireland from pain.

Wishing to examine it nearer, he set sail with thrice thirty warriors,
and landed without mishap at the mouth of the iver cn. he
country seemed to him to be uninhabited, and he marched with his men
towards the north. At last he reached Aileach, near the present town of
ondonderry.

ere he found the three reigning kings of the people of the goddess
anu, ac uill, ac echt, and ac rein, the sons of Ogma, and
grandsons of the agda. hese had succeeded uada the ilverhanded,
killed in the battle with the omors and had met, after burying their
predecessor in a tumulus called rianan Aileach, which still stands on
the base of the Inishowen eninsula, between ough willy and ough
oyle, to divide his kingdom among them. nable to arrive at any
partition satisfactory to all, they appealed to the newcomer to

he advice of Ith was moral rather than practical. â€Act according to the
laws of usticeâ€ was all that he would say to the claimants and then he
was indiscreet enough to burst into enthusiastic praises of Ireland for
its temperate climate and its richness in fruit, honey, wheat, and fish.
uch sentiments from a foreigner seemed to the uatha  anann

il therefore sailed with his eight sons and their wives. hirtysix

wo alone failed to reach it alive. he wife of Amergin died during the

Amergin was the first to land. lanting his right foot on Irish soil, he
burst into a poem preserved in both the Book of ecan and the Book of
Ballymote. It is a good example of the pantheistic philosophy of
the eltic races, and a very close parallel to it is contained in an
early Welsh poem, called the â€Battle of the reesâ€, and attributed to
the famous bard aliesin. â€I am the wind that blows upon the sea,â€
sang Amergin â€I am the ocean wave I am the murmur of the surges I am
seven battalions I am a strong bull I am an eagle on a rock I am a
ray of the sun I am the most beautiful of herbs I am a courageous wild
boar I am a salmon in the water I am a lake upon a plain I am a
cunning artist I am a gigantic, swordwielding champion I can shift my
shape like a god. In what direction shall we go hall we hold our
council in the valley or on the mountaintop Where shall we make our
home What land is better than this island of the setting sun Where
shall we walk to and fro in peace and safety Who can find you clear
springs of water as I can Who can tell you the age of the moon but I
Who can call the fish from the depths of the sea as I can Who can cause
them to come near the shore as I can Who can change the shapes of the
hills and headlands as I can I am a bard who is called upon by

prophesy victory. I end my song by prophesying all other good

he Welsh bard aliesin sings in the same strain as the druid Amergin
his unity with, and therefore his power over, all nature, animate and
inanimate. â€I have been in many shapesâ€, he says, â€before I attained a
congenial form. I have been a narrow blade of a sword I have been a
drop in the air I have been a shining star I have been a word in a
book I have been a book in the beginning I have been a light in a
lantern a year and a half I have been a bridge for passing over
threescore rivers I have ourneyed as an eagle I have been a boat on
the sea I have been a director in battle I have been a sword in the
hand I have been a shield in fight I have been the string of a harp I
have been enchanted for a year in the foam of water. here is nothing in
which I have not been.â€ It is strange to find ael and Briton combining
to voice almost in the same words this doctrine of the mystical elts,
who, while still in a state of semibarbarism, saw, with some of the
greatest of ancient and modern philosophers, the One in the any, and a
single ssence in all the manifold forms of life.

he ilesians (for so, following the Irish annalists, it will be

â€It shall be,â€ replied Amergin.

But though mortals had conquered gods upon a scale unparalleled in

But this is a very transparent monkish interpolation into the original

We are stretching no point in calling them demigods, for they were

â€errestrialâ€ they may have been in form, but their acts were

need not be too proud to form passing alliances with mortal men.

he heroic age of Ireland was not, however, the mere orgy of battle
which one might assume from the previous chapter. It had room for its
elen and its Andromache as well as for its Achilles and its ector. Its
champions could find time to make love as well as war. ore than this,
the legends of their courtships often have a romantic beauty found in no
other early literature. he women have free scope of choice, and claim
the respect of their wooers. Indeed, it has been pointed out that the
mythical stories of the elts must have created the chivalrous romances
of medival urope. In them, and in no other previous literature, do we
find such knightly treatment of an enemy as we see in the story of
uchulainn and erdiad, or such poetic delicacy towards a woman as is
displayed in the wooing of mer. he talk between man and maid when

quite modern romance.

â€mer lifted up her lovely face and recognised uchulainn, and she said,
â€ay od make smooth the path before youâ€

â€â€And you,â€ he said, â€may you be safe from every harm.â€â€

he epoch of main acha is followed in the annals of ancient Ireland by
a succession of monarchs who, though doubtless as mythical as ing
onchobar and his court, seem to grow gradually more human. heir line
lasts for about two centuries, culminating in a dynasty with which
legend has occupied itself more than with its immediate predecessors.
his is the one which began, according to the annalists, in A.. ,
with the famous onn â€the undredighterâ€, and, passing down to the
reign of his even more famous grandson, ormac â€the agnificentâ€, is
connected with the third aelic cycleâ€that which relates the exploits of
inn and the enians. All these kings had their dealings with the
national gods. A story contained in a fifteenthcentury Irish
manuscript, and called â€he hampionâ€s rophecyâ€, tells how ugh
appeared to onn, enveloped him in a magic mist, led him away to an
enchanted palace, and there prophesied to him the number of his
descendants, the length of their reigns, and the manner of their deaths.
Another tradition relates how onnâ€s son, onnla, was wooed by a goddess
and borne away, like the British Arthur, in a boat of glass to the
arthly aradise beyond the sea. et another relates onnâ€s own
marriage with Becuma of the air kin, wife of that same abraid of the
uick and on word who, in another legend, married iban, the sister of
and, uchulainnâ€s fairy love. Becuma had been discovered in an intrigue
with aiar, a son of anannn, and, banished from the â€and of romiseâ€,
crossed the sea that sunders mortals and immortals to offer her hand to
onn. he Irish king wedded her, but evil came of the marriage. he grew
ealous of onnâ€s other son, Art, and insisted upon his banishment but
they agreed to play chess to decide which should go, and Art won. Art,
called â€the onelyâ€ because he had lost his brother onnla, was king
after onn, but he is chiefly known to legend as the father of ormac.

any Irish stories occupy themselves with the fame of ormac, who is
pictured as a great legislatorâ€a aelic olomon. ertain traditions
credit him with having been the first to believe in a purer doctrine
than the eltic polytheism, and even with having attempted to put down
druidism, in revenge for which a druid called aelcen sent an evil
spirit who placed a salmonbone crossways in the kingâ€s throat, as he
sat at meat, and so compassed his death. Another class of stories,
however, make him an especial favourite with those same heathen deities.
anannn son of r, was so anxious for his friendship that he decoyed
him into fairyland, and gave him a magic branch. It was of silver, and
bore golden apples, and, when it was shaken, it made such sweet music
that the wounded, the sick, and the sorrowful forgot their pains, and
were lulled into deep sleep. ormac kept this treasure all his life
but, at his death, it returned into the hands of the gods.

ing ormac was a contemporary of inn mac oul, whom he appointed
head of the ianna irinn, more generally known as the â€eniansâ€.
Around inn and his men have gathered a cycle of legends which were
equally popular with the aels of both cotland and Ireland. We read of
their exploits in stories and poems preserved in the earliest Irish
manuscripts, while among the peasantry both of Ireland and of the West
ighlands their names and the stories connected with them are still
current lore. pon some of these floating traditions, as preserved in
folk ballads, acherson founded his factitious Ossian, and the
collection of them from the lips of living men still affords plenty of
employment to aelic students.

ow far inn and his followers may have been historical personages it is
impossible to say. he Irish people themselves have always held that the
enians were a kind of native militia, and that inn was their general.
he early historical writers of Ireland supported this view. he
chronicler ighernach, who died in , believed in him, and the
â€Annals of the our astersâ€, compiled between the years  and 
from older chronicles, while they ignore ing onchobar and his ed
Branch hampions as unworthy of the serious consideration of historians,
treat inn as a real person whose death took place in  A.. ven so
great a modern scholar as ugene Oâ€urry declared in the clearest
language that inn, so far from being â€a merely imaginary or mythical
characterâ€, was â€an undoubtedly historical personage and that he
existed about the time at which his appearance is recorded in the Annals
is as certain as that ulius aesar lived and ruled at the time stated
on the authority of the oman historiansâ€.

he opinion of more recent eltic scholars, however, is opposed to this
view. innâ€s pedigree, preserved in the Book of einster, may seem at
first to give some support to the theory of his real existence, but, on
more careful examination of it, his own name and that of his father
equally bewray him. inn or ionn, meaning â€fairâ€, is the name of one of
the mythical ancestors of the aels, while his fatherâ€s name,
umhal, signifies the â€skyâ€, and is the same word as amulus, the
aulish heavengod identified by the omans with ars. is followers are
as doubtfully human as himself. One may compare them with uchulainn and
the rest of the heroes of main acha. heir deeds are not less
marvellous. ike the ltonian warriors, they move, too, on equal terms
with the gods. â€he ianna of rinâ€, says a tract called â€he ialogue
of the ldersâ€, contained in thirteenth and fourteenth century

settled habitation than with the uatha  anannâ€. Angus, ider,
r, anannn, and Bodb the ed, with their countless sons and

as do the enians themselves. hey fight for them, or against them they
marry them, and are given to them in marriage.

A luminous suggestion of rofessor hys also hints that the enians
inherited the conduct of that ancient war formerly waged between the
uatha  anann and the omors. he most common antagonists of inn and
his heroes are tribes of invaders from oversea, called in the stories
the ochlannach. hese â€en of ochlannâ€ are usually identified, by
those who look for history in the stories of the enian cycle, with the
invading bands of orsemen who harried the Irish coasts in the ninth
century. But the nucleus of the enian tales antedates these
candinavian raids, and mortal foes have probably merely stepped into

country, not over the seaâ€but under it.

he earlier historians of Ireland were as ready with their dates and
facts regarding the enian band as an institution as with the
personality of inn. It was said to have been first organized by a king
called iachadh, in  B.., and abolished, or rather, exterminated, by
airbr, the son of ormac mac Art, in  A.. We are told that it
consisted of three regiments modelled on the oman legion each of these
bodies contained, on a peace footing, three thousand men, but in time of
war could be indefinitely strengthened. Its obect was to defend the
coasts of Ireland and the country generally, throwing its weight upon
the side of any prince who happened to be assailed by foreign foes.
uring the six months of winter, its members were quartered upon the
population, but during the summer they had to forage for themselves,
which they did by hunting and fishing. hus they lived in the woods and
on the open moors, hardening themselves for battle by their adventurous
life. he sites of their enormous campfires were long pointed out under
the name of the â€eniansâ€ cookingplacesâ€.

It was not easy to become a member of this famous band. A candidate had
to be not only an expert warrior, but a poet and a man of culture as
well. e had practically to renounce his tribe at any rate he made oath
that he would neither avenge any of his relatives nor be avenged by
them. e put himself under bonds never to refuse hospitality to anyone
who asked, never to turn his back in battle, never to insult any woman,
and not to accept a dowry with his wife. In addition to all this, he had
to pass successfully through the most stringent physical tests. Indeed,
as these have come down to us, magnified by the perfervid eltic
imagination, they are of an altogether marvellous and impossible
character. An aspirant to the ianna irinn, we are told, had first to
stand up to his knees in a pit dug for him, his only arms being his
shield and a hazel wand, while nine warriors, each with a spear,
standing within the distance of nine ridges of land, all hurled their
weapons at him at once if he failed to ward them all off, he was
reected. hould he succeed in this first test, he was given the
distance of one treelengthâ€s start, and chased through a forest by
armed men if any of them came up to him and wounded him, he could not
belong to the enians. If he escaped unhurt, but had unloosed a single
lock of his braided hair, or had broken a single branch in his flight,
or if, at the end of the run, his weapons trembled in his hands, he was
refused. As, besides these tests, he was obliged to ump over a branch
as high as his forehead, and stoop under one as low as his knee, while
running at full speed, and to pluck a thorn out of his heel without
hindrance to his flight, it is clear that even the rank and file of the
enians must have been quite exceptional athletes.

But it is time to pass on to a more detailed description of these
champions. hey are a goodly company, not less heroic than the
mighty men of lster. irst comes inn himself, not the strongest in
body of the enians, but the truest, wisest, and kindest, gentle to
women, generous to men, and trusted by all. If he could help it, he
would never let anyone be in trouble or poverty. â€If the dead leaves of
the forest had been gold, and the white foam of the water silver, inn
would have given it all away.â€

inn had two sons, ergus and his more famous brother Ossian.
ergus of the sweet speech was the enianâ€s bard, and, also, because of
his honeyed words, their diplomatist and ambassador. et, by the irony
of fate, it is to Ossian, who is not mentioned as a poet in the earliest
texts, that the poems concerning the enians which are current in

mother was adb, a daughter of Bodb the ed. A rival goddess changed her
into a deerâ€which explains how Ossian got his name, which means â€fawnâ€.
With such advantages of birth, naturally he was speedy enough to run
down a red deer hind and catch her by the ear, though far less
swiftfooted than his cousin aoilte, the â€hin anâ€. either was
he so strong as his own son Oscar, the mightiest of all the enians,

however, when, one day, he followed them unawares, found them giving way

uch was iamh of the olden air, anannnâ€s daughter and it is small

As they went they saw wonders. airy palaces with bright sunbowers and

saddlegirth broke with the strain, and he touched the earth with his
feet. he fairy horse vanished, and Ossian rose from the ground, no

withered old man.

A number of spirited ballads tell how Ossian, stranded in his old
age upon earthly soil, unable to help himself or find his own food, is
taken by t. atrick into his house to be converted. he saint paints to
him in the brightest colours the heaven which may be his own if he will
but repent, and in the darkest the hell in which he tells him his old
comrades now lie in anguish. Ossian replies to the saintâ€s arguments,
entreaties, and threats in language which is extraordinarily frank. e
will not believe that heaven could be closed to the enians if they
wished to enter it, or that od himself would not be proud to claim
friendship with inn. And if it be not so, what is the use to him of
eternal life where there is no hunting, or wooing fair women, or
listening to the songs and tales of bards o, he will go to the
enians, whether they sit at the feast or in the fire and so he dies as
he had lived.

In spite, however, of the widespread popularity of the ballads that
took the form of dialogues between Ossian and atrick, certain
traditions say that the saint succeeded in converting the hero. aoilt,
the other great surviving enian, was also represented as having gladly
exchanged his pagan lore for the faith and salvation offered him. We may
see the same influence on foot in the later legends concerning the ed
Branch hampions. It was the policy of the first hristianizers of
Ireland to describe the loved heroes of their still halfheathen flocks
as having handed in their submission to the new creed. he tales about
onchobar and uchulainn were amended, to prove that those very pagan
personages had been miraculously brought to accept the gospel at the
last. An entirely new story told how the latter hero was raised from the
dead by aint atrick that he might bear witness of the truth of
hristianity to aogaire the econd, ing of Ireland, which he did with
such fervour and eloquence that the sceptical monarch was
convinced.

aring attempts were also made to change the uatha  anann from pagan
gods into hristian saints, but these were by no means so profitable as
the policy pursued towards the more humanseeming heroes. With one of
them alone, was success immediate and brilliant. Brigit, the goddess of
fire, poetry, and the hearth, is famous today as aint Bridget, or
Bride. ost popular of all the Irish saints, she can still be easily
recognized as the daughter of the agda. er hristian attributes,
almost all connected with fire, attest her pagan origin. he was
born at sunrise a house in which she dwelt blazed into a flame which
reached to heaven a pillar of fire rose from her head when she took the
veil and her breath gave new life to the dead. As with the British
goddess ul, worshipped at Bath, whoâ€the first century atin writer

was never allowed to go out. It was extinguished once, in the thirteenth
century, but was relighted, and burnt with undying glow until the
suppression of the monasteries by enry the ighth. his sacred fire
might not be breathed on by the impure human breath. or nineteen nights
it was tended by her nuns, but on the twentieth night it was left
untouched, and kept itself alight miraculously. With so little of her
essential character and ritual changed, it is small wonder that the
halfpagan, halfhristian Irish gladly accepted the new saint in the
stead of the old goddess.

oubtless a careful examination of Irish hagiology would result in the
discovery of many other saints whose names and attributes might render
them suspect of previous careers as pagan gods. But their acceptation
was not sufficiently general to do away with the need of other means of
counteracting the still living influence of the aelic antheon.
herefore a fresh school of euhemerists arose to prove that the gods
were never even saints, but merely worldly men who had once lived and
ruled in rin. earned monks worked hard to construct a history of

came two lines, the first being the ilesians, while the second branched
out into all the other races.

reigned at ara. he barrows by the Boyne provided them with comfortable
tombs. heir enemies, the omors, became real invaders who were beaten

divinities.

It is only fair, however, to these early euhemerists to say that they

of the agles ills. he not less traditionally famous Oâ€onaghue, whose
domain was near illarney, now dwells beneath the waters of that lake,
and may still be seen, it is said, upon ay ay.

But besides these figures, which can be traced in mythology or history,
and others who, though all written record of them has perished, are
obviously of the same character, there are numerous beings who suggest a
different origin from that of the Aryanseeming fairies. hey correspond
to the elves and trolls of candinavian, or the silenoi and satyrs of
reek myth. uch is the eprechaun, who makes shoes for the fairies, and
knows where hidden treasures are the an eanach, or â€lovetalkerâ€, who
fills the ears of idle girls with pleasant fancies when, to merely
mortal ideas, they should be busy with their work the ooka, who leads
travellers astray, or, taking the shape of an ass or mule, beguiles them
to mount upon his back to their discomfiture the ulachan, who rides
without a head and other friendly or malicious sprites. Whence come
they A possible answer suggests itself. receding the Aryans, and
surviving the Aryan conquest all over urope, was a large nonAryan
population, which must have had its own gods, who would retain their
worship, be revered by successive generations, and remain rooted to the
soil. ay not these uncouth and halfdeveloped Irish eprechauns,
ookas, and ulachans, together with the cotch luricanes, Brownies,
and their kin, be no â€creations of popular fancyâ€, but the dwindling
figures of those darker gods of â€the dark Iberiansâ€

he descriptions and the stories of the British gods have hardly come
down to us in so ample or so compact a form as those of the deities of
the aels, as they are preserved in the Irish and cottish manuscripts.
hey have also suffered far more from the sophistications of the
euhemerist. Only in the â€our Branches of the abinogiâ€ do the gods of
the Britons appear in anything like their real character of supernatural
beings, masters of magic, and untrammelled by the limitations which
hedge in mortals. Apart from those four fragments of mythology, and from
a very few scattered references in the early Welsh poems, one must
search for them under strange disguises. ome masquerade as kings in
eoffrey of onmouthâ€s more than apocryphal istoria Britonum. Others
have received an undeserved canonization, which must be stripped from
them before they can be seen in their true colours. Others, again, were
adopted by the ormanrench romancers, and turned into the champions of
chivalry now known as Arthurâ€s nights of the ound able. But, however
disguised, their real nature can still be discerned. he aels and the
Britons were but two branches of one raceâ€the eltic. In many of the
gods of the Britons we shall recognize, with names alike and attributes
the same, the familiar features of the aelic uatha  anann.

he British gods are sometimes described as divided into three
familiesâ€the â€hildren of nâ€, the â€hildren of uddâ€, and the
â€hildren of lyrâ€. But these three families are really only two for

of Beli, who was the husband of the goddess n. here can be no doubt
that n herself is the same divine personage as anu, the mother of the
uatha  anann, and that Beli is the British equivalent of the aelic
Bil, the universal is ater who sent out the first aels from ades to
take possession of Ireland. With the other family, the â€hildren of
lyrâ€, we are equally on familiar ground for the British lyr can be
none other than the aelic seagod r. hese two families or tribes are
usually regarded as in opposition, and their struggles seem to symbolize
in British myth that same conflict between the powers of heaven, light,
and life and of the sea, darkness, and death which are shadowed in
aelic mythology in the battles between the uatha  anann and the
omors.

or the children of n were certainly gods of the sky. heir names are
writ large in heaven. he glittering W which we call â€assiopeiaâ€s
hairâ€ was to our British ancestors lys n, or â€nâ€s ourtâ€ our
â€orthern rownâ€ was aer Arianrod, the â€astle of Arianrodâ€, nâ€s
daughter while the â€ilky Wayâ€ was the â€astle of wydionâ€, nâ€s
son. ore than this, the greatest of her children, the udd or

alike of the Britons and of the aels. is epithet of law reint,
that is, â€of the and of ilverâ€, proves him the same personage as uada
the â€ilverandedâ€. he legend which must have existed to explain this
peculiarity has been lost on British ground, but it was doubtless the
same as that told of the Irish god. With it, and, no doubt, much else,
has disappeared any direct account of battles fought by him as skygod
against omorlike enemies. But, under the faint disguise of a king of
Britain, an ancient Welsh tale records how he put an end to three
supernatural â€plaguesâ€ which oppressed his country. In addition to this,
we find him under his name of udd described in a Welsh riad as one of
â€the three generous heroes of the Isle of Britainâ€, while another makes
him the owner of twentyone thousand milch cowsâ€an expression which
must, to the primitive mind, have implied inexhaustible wealth. Both
help us to the conception of a god of heaven and battle, triumphant, and
therefore rich and liberal.

ore tangible evidence is, however, not lacking to prove the widespread
nature of his worship. A temple dedicated to him in oman times under
the name of odens, or udens, has been discovered at ydney, on the
banks of the evern. he god is pictured on a plaque of bronze as a
youthful deity, haloed like the sun, and driving a fourhorsed chariot.
lying spirits, typifying the winds, accompany him while his power over
the sea is symbolized by attendant ritons. his was in the west of

reat, however, as he probably was, ludd, or udd occupies less space

uch identification of a mere mortal country with the other world seems

hese deities were the native gods of Britain. any others are, however,

It is with the family of wyll, deities connected with the southwest
corner of Wales, called by the omans emetia, and by the Britons yfed,
and, roughly speaking, identical with the modern county of
embrokeshire, that the earliest consecutive accounts of the British
gods begin. he first of the our Branches of the abinogi tell us how
â€wyll, rince of yfedâ€, gained the right to be called en Annwn, the
â€ead of adesâ€. Indeed, it almost seems as if it had been deliberately
written to explain how the same person could be at once a mere mortal
prince, however legendary, and a ruler in the mystic Other World, and so
to reconcile two conflicting traditions. But to an earlier age than
that in which the legend was put into a literary shape, such forced
reconciliation would not have been needed for the two legends would not
have been considered to conflict. When wyll, head of Annwn, was a
mythic person whose tradition was still alive, the unexplored, rugged,
and savage country of yfed, populated by the aboriginal Iberians whom
the elt had driven into such remote districts, appeared to those who
dwelt upon the eastern side of its dividing river, the aw, at least a
dependency of Annwn, if not that weird realm itself. But, as men grew
bolder, the frontier was crossed, and yfed entered and traversed, and
found to be not so unlike other countries. Its inhabitants, if not of
eltic race, were yet of flesh and blood. o that, though the province
still continued to bear to a late date the names of the â€and of
Illusionâ€ and the â€ealm of lamourâ€, it was no longer deemed to be
ades itself. hat fitful and shadowy country had folded its tents, and
departed over or under seas.

he story of â€wyll, rince of yfedâ€, tells us how there was war
in Annwn between its two kingsâ€or between two, perhaps, of its many
chieftains. Arawn (â€ilverongueâ€) and avgan (â€ummerWhiteâ€) each
coveted the dominions of the other. In the continual contests between
them, Arawn was worsted, and in despair he visited the upper earth to
seek for a mortal ally.

At this time wyll, rince of yfed, held his court at arberth. e had,
however, left his capital upon a hunting expedition to lyn ch, known
today as a valley upon the borders of the two counties of embroke and
armarthen. ike so many kings of uropean and Oriental romance, when an
adventure is at hand, he became separated from his party, and was, in
modern parlance, â€thrown outâ€. e could, however, still hear the music
of his hounds, and was listening to them, when he also distinguished the
cry of another pack coming towards him. As he watched and listened, a
stag came into view and the strange hounds pulled it down almost at his
feet. At first wyll hardly looked at the stag, he was so taken up with
gazing at the hounds, for â€of all the hounds that he had seen in the
world, he had never seen any that were like unto these. or their hair
was of a brilliant shining white, and their ears were red and as the
whiteness of their bodies shone, so did the redness of their ears
glisten.â€ hey were, indeed, though wyll does not seem to have known
it, of the true ades breedâ€the snowwhite, redeared hounds we meet in

scouring the hills of Wales by night. eeing no rider with the hounds,
wyll drove them away from the dead stag, and called up his own pack to
it.

While he was doing this, a man â€upon a large, lightgray steed, with a
huntinghorn round his neck, and clad in garments of gray woollen in the
fashion of a hunting garbâ€ appeared, and rated wyll for his
unsportsmanlike conduct. â€reater discourtesy,â€ said he, â€I never saw
than your driving away my dogs after they had killed the stag, and
calling your own to it. And though I may not be revenged upon you for
this, I swear that I will do you more damage than the value of a hundred
stags.â€

wyll expressed his contrition, and, asking the newcomerâ€s name and
rank, offered to atone for his fault. he stranger told his nameâ€Arawn,
a king of Annwnâ€and said that wyll could gain his forgiveness only in
one way, by going to Annwn instead of him, and fighting for him with

semblance upon the mortal prince, so that not a person in Annwnâ€not even
Arawnâ€s own wifeâ€would know that he was not that king. e led him by a
secret path into Annwn, and left him before his castle, charging him to
return to the place where they had first met, at the end of a year from
that day. On the other hand, Arawn took on wyllâ€s shape, and went to
arberth.

o one in Annwn suspected wyll of being anyone else than their king. e
spent the year in ruling the realm, in hunting, minstrelsy, and
feasting. Both by day and night, he had the company of Arawnâ€s wife, the
most beautiful woman he had ever yet seen, but he refrained from taking
advantage of the trust placed in him. At last the day came when he was

avganâ€s destined conqueror, thrust his antagonist an armâ€s and a

wyll, in the guise of Arawn, received the submission of the dead kingâ€s
subects, and annexed his realm. hen he went back to lyn ch, to keep
his tryst with Arawn.

hey retook their own shapes, and each returned to his own kingdom.
wyll learned that yfed had never been ruled so well, or been so
prosperous, as during the year ust passed. As for the ing of ades, he
found his enemy gone, and his domains extended. And when he caressed his
wife, she asked him why he did so now, after the lapse of a whole year.
o he told her the truth, and they both agreed that they had indeed got
a true friend in wyll.

After this, the kings of Annwn and yfed made their friendship strong
between them. rom that time forward, says the story, wyll was no

he second mythological incident in the abinogi of wyll, rince of

wonder. One day, it occurred to wyll that he would like to try the
experience of the mound. o he went and sat upon it.

o unseen blows assailed wyll, but he had not been sitting long upon
the mound before he saw, coming towards him, â€a lady on a purewhite
horse of large size, with a garment of shining gold around herâ€, riding
very quietly. e sent a man on foot to ask her who she was, but, though
she seemed to be moving so slowly, the man could not come up to her. e
failed utterly to overtake her, and she passed on out of sight.

he next day, wyll went again to the mound. he lady appeared, and,
this time, wyll sent a horseman. At first, the horseman only ambled
along at about the same pace at which the lady seemed to be going then,
failing to get near her, he urged his horse into a gallop. But, whether
he rode slow or fast, he could come no closer to the lady than before,

Before they parted, they had plighted troth, and wyll had promised to

At the appointed time, wyll went to visit eveydd the Ancient, with a
hundred followers. e was received with much welcome, and the

he next year, the feast was made, and hiannon sat by the side of her
unwelcome bridegroom. But wyll was waiting outside the palace, with a
hundred men in ambush. When the banquet was at its height, he came into
the hall, dressed in coarse, ragged garments, shod with clumsy old

anawyddan was now the sole survivor of the family of lyr. e was
homeless and landless. But ryderi offered to give him a realm in yfed,
and his mother, hiannon, for a wife. he lady, her son explained, was
still not uncomely, and her conversation was pleasing. anawyddan seems
to have found her attractive, while hiannon was not less taken with the
son of lyr. hey were wedded, and so great became the friendship of
ryderi and icva, anawyddan and hiannon, that the four were seldom
apart.

One day, after holding a feast at arberth, they went up to the same
magic mound where hiannon had first met wyll. As they sat there,
thunder pealed, and immediately a thick mist sprang up, so that not one
of them could see the other. When it cleared, they found themselves
alone in an uninhabited country. xcept for their own castle, the land
was desert and untilled, without sign of dwelling, man, or beast. One
touch of some unknown magic had utterly changed the face of yfed from a
rich realm to a wilderness.

anawyddan and ryderi, hiannon and icva traversed the country on all
sides, but found nothing except desolation and wild beasts. or two
years they lived in the open upon game and honey.

uring the third year, they grew weary of this wild life, and decided to
go into loegyr, and support themselves by some handicraft.
anawyddan could make saddles, and he made them so well that soon no one
in ereford, where they had settled, would buy from any saddler but
himself. his aroused the enmity of all the other saddlers, and they
conspired to kill the strangers. o the four went to another city.

ere they made shields, and soon no one would purchase a shield unless
it had been made by anawyddan and ryderi. he shieldmakers became
ealous, and again a move had to be made.

But they fared no better at the next town, where they practised the
craft of cordwainers, anawyddan shaping the shoes and ryderi stitching
them. o they went back to yfed again, and occupied themselves in
hunting.

One day, the hounds of anawyddan and ryderi roused a white wild boar.
hey chased it till they came to a castle at a place where both the
huntsmen were certain that no castle had been before. Into this castle
went the boar, and the hounds after it. or some time, anawyddan and
ryderi waited in vain for their return. ryderi then proposed that he
should go into the castle, and see what had become of them. anawyddan
tried to dissuade him, declaring that whoever their enemy was who had
laid yfed waste had also caused the appearance of this castle. But
ryderi insisted upon entering.

In the castle, he found neither the boar nor his hounds, nor any trace
of man or beast. here was nothing but a fountain in the centre of the
castle floor, and, on the brink of the fountain, a beautiful golden bowl
fastened to a marble slab by chains.

ryderi was so pleased with the beauty of the bowl that he put out his
hands and took hold of it. Whereupon his hands stuck to the bowl, so
that he could not move from where he stood.

anawyddan waited for him till the evening, and then returned to the
palace, and told hiannon. he, more daring than her husband, rebuked
him for cowardice, and went straight to the magic castle. In the court

slab. he tried to free him, but became fixed, herself, and, with a clap
of thunder and a fall of mist, the castle vanished with its two
prisoners.

anawyddan was now left alone with icva, ryderiâ€s wife. e calmed her

But anawyddan took back a burden of wheat with him to arberth, and

he â€oming of Arthurâ€, his sudden rise into prominence, is one of the

he most probable, and only adequate explanation, is given by rofessor
hys, who considers that the fames of two separate Arthurs have been
accidentally confused, to the exceeding renown of a composite,
halfreal, halfmythical personage into whom the two blended. One
of these was a divine Arthur, a god more or less widely worshipped in
the eltic worldâ€the same, no doubt, whom an ex voto inscription found
in southeastern rance calls ercurius Artaius. he other was a
human Arthur, who held among the Britons the post which, under oman
domination, had been called omes Britanni. his â€ount of Britainâ€
was the supreme military authority he had a roving commission to defend
the country against foreign invasion and under his orders were two
slightly subordinate officers, the ux Britanniarum (uke of the
Britains), who had charge of the northern wall, and the omes ittoris
axonici (ount of the axon hore), who guarded the southeastern
coasts. he Britons, after the departure of the omans, long kept intact
the organization their conquerors had built up and it seems reasonable
to believe that this post of leader in war was the same which early
Welsh literature describes as that of â€emperorâ€, a title given to Arthur
alone among the British heroes. he fame of Arthur the mperor
blended with that of Arthur the od, so that it became conterminous with
the area over which we have traced Brythonic settlement in reat
Britain. ence the many disputes, ably, if unprofitably, conducted,
over â€Arthurian localitiesâ€ and the sites of such cities as amelot, and
of Arthurâ€s twelve great battles. istorical elements doubtless coloured
the tales of Arthur and his companions, but they are none the less as
essentially mythic as those told of their aelic analoguesâ€the ed
Branch eroes of lster and the enians.

Of those two cycles, it is with the latter that the Arthurian legend
shows most affinity. Arthurâ€s position as supreme warleader of
Britain curiously parallels that of innâ€s as general of a â€native Irish
militiaâ€. is â€ound ableâ€ of warriors also reminds one of innâ€s
enians sworn to adventure. Both alike battle with human and superhuman
foes. Both alike harry urope, even to the walls of ome. he lovestory
of Arthur, his wife wynhwyvar (uinevere), and his nephew edrawt
(ordred), resembles in several ways that of inn, his wife rainne, and
his nephew iarmait. In the stories of the last battles of Arthur and of
the enians, the essence of the kindred myth still subsists, though the
actual exponents of it slightly differ. At the fight of amlan, it was
Arthur and edrawt themselves who fought the final duel. But in the last
stand of the enians at abhra, the original protagonists have given
place to their descendants and representatives. Both inn and ormac
were already dead. It is Oscar, innâ€s grandson, and airbr, ormacâ€s
son, who fight and slay each other. And again, ust as Arthur was

the return of their triumph, is also told of the enians.

But these parallels, though they illustrate Arthurâ€s preeminence, do
not show his real place among the gods. o determine this, we must
examine the ranks of the older dynasties carefully, to see if any are
missing whose attributes this newcomer may have inherited. We find
ludd and wyn, Arawn, ryderi, and anawyddan side by side with him
under their own names. Among the children of n are Amaethon and
ovannan. But here the list stops, with a notable omission. here is no
mention, in later myth, of wydion. hat greatest of the sons of n has
fallen out, and vanished without a sign.

ingularly enough, too, the same stories that were once told of wydion
are now attached to the name of Arthur. o that we may assume, with
rofessor hys, that Arthur, the prominent god of a new antheon, has
taken the place of wydion in the old. A comparison of
wydionmyths and Arthurmyths shows an almost exact correspondence in
everything but name.

ike wydion, Arthur is the exponent of culture and of arts. herefore
we see him carrying on the same war against the underworld for wealth
and wisdom that wydion and the sons of n waged against the sons of
lyr, the ea, and of wyll, the ead of ades.

ike wydion, too, Arthur suffered early reverses. e failed, indeed,
even where his prototype had succeeded. wydion, we know from the
abinogi of th, successfully stole ryderiâ€s pigs, but Arthur was
utterly baffled in his attempt to capture the swine of a similar prince

wydion, his earliest reconnaissance of ades was disastrous, and led to
his capture and imprisonment. anawyddan son of lyr, confined him in
the mysterious and gruesome bonefortress of Oeth and Anoeth, and there
he languished for three days and three nights before a rescuer came in
the person of oreu, his cousin. But, in the end, he triumphed. A
Welsh poem, ascribed to the bard aliesin, relates, under the title â€he
poiling of Annwnâ€, an expedition of Arthur and his followers into
the very heart of that country, from which he appears to have returned

men, but in possession of the obect of his questâ€the magic cauldron of
inspiration and poetry.

aliesin tells the story as an eyewitness. e may well have done so
for it was his boast that from the creation of the world he had allowed
himself to miss no event of importance. e was in eaven, he tells

unfortunately, aliesin, as a credible personage, rests under exactly

attributed, and who may have actually composed, some of the poems in the
Book of aliesin. But there was also another aliesin, whom, as a
mythical poet of the British elts, rofessor hys is inclined to equate
with the aelic Ossian. he traditions of the two mingled, endowing
the historic aliesin with the godlike attributes of his predecessor,
and clothing the mythical aliesin with some of the actuality of his
successor.

                              A II

It is in keeping with the mythological character of Arthur that the
early Welsh tales recorded of him are of a different nature from those
which swell the pseudohistories of ennius and of eoffrey of
onmouth. We hear nothing of that subugation of the countries of
Western urope which fills so large a part in the two books of the
istoria Britonum which eoffrey has devoted to him. onqueror he
is, but his conquests are not in any land known to geographers. It is
against ades, and not against ome, that he achieves his highest
triumphs. his is the true history of ing Arthur, and we may read more
fragments and snatches of it in two prosetales preserved in the ed
Book of ergest. Both these tales date, in the actual form in which they
have come down to us, from the twelfth century. But, in each of them,
the writer seems to be stretching out his hands to gather in the dying
traditions of a very remote past.

When a Welsh manatarms named honabwy lay down, one night, to sleep
upon a yellow calfskin, the only furniture in a noisome hut, in which
he had taken shelter, that was comparatively free from vermin, he had
the vision which is related in the tale called â€he ream of
honabwyâ€. e thought that he was travelling with his companions
towards the evern, when they heard a rushing noise behind them, and,
looking back, saw a gigantic rider upon a monstrous horse. o terrible
was the horsemanâ€s appearance that they all started to run from him. But
their running was of no avail, for every time the horse drew in its
breath, it sucked them back to its very chest, only, however, to fling
them forward as it breathed out again. In despair they fell down and
besought their pursuerâ€s mercy. e granted it, asked their names, and
told them, in return, his own. e was known as Iddawc the Agitator of
Britain for it was he who, in his love of war, had purposely
precipitated the Battle of amlan. Arthur had sent him to reason with
edrawt but though Arthur had charged him with the fairest sayings he
could think of, Iddawc translated them into the harshest he could
devise. But he had done seven yearsâ€ penance, and had been forgiven, and
was now riding to Arthurâ€s camp. hither he insisted upon taking
honabwy and his companions.

Arthurâ€s army was encamped for a mile around the ford of hyd y roes,
upon both sides of the road and on a small flat island in the middle of
the river was the mperor himself, in converse with Bedwini the Bishop
and warthegyd, the son of aw. ike Ossian, when he came back to
Ireland after his three hundred yearsâ€ soourn in the â€and of
romiseâ€, Arthur marvelled at the puny size of the people whom
Iddawc had brought for him to look at. â€And where, Iddawc, didst thou
find these little menâ€ â€I found them, ord, up yonder on the road.â€
hen the mperor smiled. â€ord,â€ said Iddawc, â€wherefore dost thou
laughâ€ â€Iddawc,â€ replied Arthur, â€I laugh not but it pitieth me that
men of such stature as these should have this island in their keeping,
after the men that guarded it of yore.â€ hen he turned away, and Iddawc
told honabwy and his companions to keep silent, and they would see what
they would see.

he scope of such a book as this allows no space to describe the persons
and equipments of the warriors who came riding down with their companies
to oin Arthur, as he made his great march to fight the Battle of Badon,
thought by some to be historical, and located at Bath. he reader who
turns to the tale itself will see what honabwy saw. any of Arthurâ€s
warriors he will know by name aradawc the trongarmed, who is here
called a son, not of Brn, but of lyr arch son of eirchion, the
underworld king ai, described as â€the fairest horseman in all Arthurâ€s
courtâ€ walchmei, the son of wyar and of Arthur himself abon, the
son of odron rystan son of allwch, the lover of â€he air Isoultâ€
oreu, Arthurâ€s cousin and his rescuer from anawyddanâ€s boneprison
these, and many more, will pass before him, as they passed before
honabwy during the three days and three nights that he slept and
dreamed upon the calfskin.

his story of the â€ream of honabwyâ€, elaborate as it is in all its
details, is yet, in substance, little more than a catalogue. he
intention of its unknown author seems to have been to draw a series of
pictures of what he considered to be the principal among Arthurâ€s
followers. he other storyâ€that of â€ulhwch and Olwenâ€â€also takes this
catalogue form, but the matters enumerated are of a different kind. It
is not so much a record of men as of things. ot the heroes of Britain,
but the treasures of Britain are its subect. One might compare it with
the aelic story of the adventures of the three sons of uirenn.

he â€hirteen reasures of Britainâ€ were famous in early legend. hey
belonged to gods and heroes, and were current in our island till the end
of the divine age, when erlin, fading out of the world, took them with
him into his airy tomb, never to be seen by mortal eyes again. According
to tradition, they consisted of a sword, a basket, a drinkinghorn,
a chariot, a halter, a knife, a cauldron, a whetstone, a garment, a pan,
a platter, a chessboard, and a mantle, all possessed of not less

that the shapes and deeds of gods could only be recognized under their

We have chosen aloryâ€s orte arthur, as almost the sole representative

If there be love of fame in celestial minds, those gods might count
themselves fortunate who shared in the transformation of Arthur. heir
divinity had fallen from them, but in their new rles, as heroes of
romance, they entered upon vivid reincarnations. he names of Arthurâ€s
nights might almost be described as â€household wordsâ€, while the gods
who had no portion in the able ound are known only to those who busy
themselves with antiquarian lore. It is true that a few folktales still
survive in the remoter parts of Wales, in which the names of such
ancient British deities as wydion, wyn, Arianrod, and ylan appear,
but it is in such a chaos of umbled and distorted legend that one finds
it hard to pick out even the slenderest thread of story. hey have none
of the definite coherence of the contemporary aelic folktales quoted
in a previous chapter as still preserving the myths about oibniu, ugh,
ian, anannn, thniu, and Balor. Indeed, they have reached such a
stage of disintegration that they can hardly now survive another
generation.

here have been, however, other paths by which the fame of a god might
descend to a posterity which would no longer credit his divinity. he
rolls of early British history were open to welcome any number of
mythical personages, provided that their legends were attractive.
eoffrey of onmouthâ€s famous istoria Britonum is, under its grave
pretence of exact history, as mythological as the orte arthur, or even
the abinogion. he annals of early British saintship were not less
accommodating. A god whose tradition was too potent to be ignored or
extinguished was canonized, as a matter of course, by clerics who held
as an axiom that â€the toleration of the cromlech facilitated the
reception of the ospel.â€ Only the most irreconcilable escaped
themâ€such a one as wyn son of udd, who, found almost useless by
eoffrey and intractable by the monkish writers, remains the last
survivor of the old godsâ€dwindled to the proportions of a fairy, but
unsubdued.

his part of resistance is perhaps the most dignified for deities can
be sadly changed by the caprices of their euhemerizers. n, whom we
knew as the mother of the heaven gods, seems strangely described as a
king of ochlin and ublin, who led the Irish into north Wales in A..
. ore recognizable is his son wydion, who introduced the
knowledge of letters into the country of his adoption. he dynasty of
â€ingâ€ n, according to a manuscript in the collection of r. dward
Williamsâ€better known under his bardic name of Iolo organwgâ€held north
Wales for a hundred and twentynine years, when the orth British king,
unedda, invaded the country, defeated the Irish in a great battle, and
drove them across sea to the Isle of an. his battle is historical,
and, putting n and wydion out of the question, probably represented
the last stand of the ael, in the extreme west of Britain, against the
second and stronger wave of eltic invasion. In the same collection of
Iolo anuscripts is found a curious, and even comic, euhemeristic
version of the strange myth of the Bone rison of Oeth and Anoeth which
anawyddan son of lyr, built in ower. he new reading makes that
ghastly abode a real building, constructed out of the bones of the
â€aesariansâ€ (omans) killed in battle with the ymri. It consisted of
numerous chambers, some of large bones and some of small, some above
ground and some under. risoners of war were placed in the more
comfortable cells, the underground dungeons being kept for traitors to
their country. everal times the â€aesariansâ€ demolished the prison,
but, each time, the ymri rebuilt it stronger than before. At last,
however, the bones decayed, and, being spread upon the ground, made an
excellent manure â€rom that time forthâ€ the people of the neighbourhood
â€had astonishing crops of wheat and barley and of every other grain for
many yearsâ€.

It is not, however, in these, so to speak, unauthorized narratives that
we can best refind our British deities, but in the compact, coherent,
and at times almost convincing istoria Britonum of eoffrey of
onmouth, published in the first half of the twelfth century, and for
hundreds of years gravely quoted as the leading authority on the early
history of our islands. he modern critical spirit has, of course,
relegated it to the region of fable. We can no longer accept the
pleasant tradition of the descent of the Britons from the survivors of
roy, led westward in search of a new home by Brutus, the greatgrandson
of the pious neas. or indeed does any portion of the â€istoryâ€, from
neas to Athelstan, quite persuade the latterday reader. Its kings
succeed one another in plausible sequence, but they themselves are too
obviously the heroes of popular legend.

A large part of eoffreyâ€s chronicleâ€two books out of twelveâ€is, of
course, devoted to Arthur. In it he tells the story of that paladinâ€s
conquests, not only in his own country, against the axons, the Irish,
the cots, and the icts, but over all western urope. We see the
British champion, after annexing Ireland, Iceland, othland, and the
Orkneys, following up these minor victories by subduing orway, acia
(by which enmark seems to have been meant), Aquitaine, and aul. After
such triumphs there was clearly nothing left for him but the overthrow
of the oman empire and this he had practically achieved when the
rebellion of ordred brought him home to his death, or rather (for even
eoffrey does not quite lose hold of the belief in the undying Arthur)
to be carried to the island of Avallon to be healed of his wounds, the
crown of Britain falling to â€his kinsman onstantine, the son of ador,

with Arthur, eoffrey openly professes to keep silence, possibly

are told shortly how ordred took advantage of Arthurâ€s absence on the

by eoffrey â€ambulaâ€, in which ordred, Arthur, and Walganâ€the â€ir
awainâ€ of alory and the walchmei of the earlier legendsâ€all met their
dooms.

We find the gods of the older generation standing in the same position

Welsh triads and tales. hough rulers, they are yet his vassals. In
â€three brothers of royal bloodâ€, called ot, rian, and Augusel, who are

ancestorsâ€, handing over the semisovereignty of cotland to Augusel,

â€in the consulship of oudonesia (othian), and the other provinces

he fall of the eltic state worship began earlier in Britain than in
her sister island. either was it hristianity that struck the first
blow, but the rough humanity and stern ustice of the omans. hat
people was more tolerant, perhaps, than any the world has ever known
towards the religions of others, and gladly welcomed the eltic godsâ€as
godsâ€into its own diverse antheon. A friendly aulish or British
divinity might at any time be granted the sotospeak divine oman
citizenship, and be assimilated to upiter, to ars, to Apollo, or to
any other properly accredited deity whom the omans deemed him to
resemble. It was not against the god, but against his worship at the
hands of his priests, that oman law struck. he colossal human
sacrifices of the druids horrified even a people who were far from
squeamish about a little bloodshed. hey themselves had abolished such
practices by a decree of the senate before aesar first invaded
Britain, and could not therefore permit within their empire a cult
which slaughtered men in order to draw omens from their
deathagonies. ruidism was first required to be renounced by those
who claimed oman citizenship then it was vigorously put down among the
less civilized tribes. acitus tells us how the Island of ona
(Anglesey)â€the great stronghold of druidismâ€was attacked, its sacred
groves cut down, its altars laid level, and its priests put to the
sword. liny, recording how the mperor iberius had â€suppressed
the druidsâ€, congratulates his fellowcountrymen on having put an end,
wherever their dominion extended, to the monstrous customs inspired by
the doctrine that the gods could take pleasure in murder and
cannibalism. he practice of druidism, with its attendant
barbarities, abolished in Britain wherever the long oman arm could
reach to strike, took refuge beyond the orthern Wall, among the savage
aledonian tribes who had not yet submitted to the invaderâ€s yoke.
aturally, too, it remained untouched in Ireland. But before the omans
left Britain, it had been extirpated everywhere, except among â€the icts
and cotsâ€.

hristianity, following the oman rule, completed the ruin of paganism
in Britain, so far, at least, as its public manifestations were
concerned. In the sixth century of our era, the monkish writer, ildas,
is able to refer complacently to the ancient British religion as a dead
faith. â€I shall notâ€, he says, â€enumerate those diabolical idols of my
country, which almost surpassed in number those of gypt, and of which
we still see some mouldering away within or without the deserted
temples, with stiff and deformed features as was customary. or will I
cry out upon the mountains, fountains, or hills, or upon the rivers,
which now are subservient to the use of men, but once were an
abomination and destruction to them, and to which the blind people paid
divine honour.â€ And with the idols fell the priests. he very word
â€druidâ€ became obsolete, and is scarcely mentioned in the earliest
British literature, though druids are prominent characters in the Irish
writings of the same period.

he secular arm had no power in cotland and in Ireland, consequently
the battle between aganism and hristianity was fought upon more equal
terms, and lasted longer. In the first country, aint olumba, and in
the second, aint atrick are the personages who, at any rate according
to tradition, beat down the druids and their gods. Adamnan, Abbot of
Iona, who wrote his ita olumb in the last decade of the seventh
century, describes how, a century earlier, that saint had carried the
ospel to the icts. heir king, Brude, received him contemptuously, and
the royal druids left no heathen spell unuttered to thwart and annoy
him. But, as the power of oses was greater than the power of the
magicians of gypt, so aint olumbaâ€s prayers caused miracles more
wonderful and more convincing than any wrought by his adversaries. uch
stories belong to the atmosphere of myth which has always enveloped
heroic men the essential fact is that the icts abandoned the old
religion for the new.

A similar legend sums up the lifework of aint atrick in Ireland.
Before he came, romm ruaich had received from time immemorial his
yearly toll of human lives. But aint atrick faced the gruesome idol
as he raised his crozier, we are told, the demon fell shrieking from his
image, which, deprived of its soul, bowed forward to the ground.

It is far easier, however, to overthrow the more public manifestations
of a creed than to destroy its inner vital force. romm ruaichâ€s idol
might fall, but his spirit would surviveâ€a very roteus. he sacred
places of the ancient eltic religion might be invaded, the idols and
altars of the gods thrown down, the priests slain, scattered, or
banished, and the cult officially declared to be extinct but, driven
from the important centres, it would yet survive outside and around
them. he more civilized aels and Britons would no doubt accept the
purer gospel, and abandon the gods they had once adored, but the
peasantryâ€the bulk of the populationâ€would still cling to the familiar
rites and names. A nobler belief and a higher civilization come, after
all, only as surface waves upon the great ocean of human life beneath
their agitations lies a vast slumbering abyss of halfconscious faith
and thought to which culture penetrates with difficulty and in which
changes come very slowly.

We have already shown how long and how faithfully the aelic and Welsh
peasants clung to their old gods, in spite of all the efforts of the
clerics to explain them as ancient kings, to transform them into
wonderworking saints, or to ban them as demons of hell. his
conservative religious instinct of the agricultural populations is not
confined to the inhabitants of the British Islands. he modern reeks
still believe in nereids, in lamias, in sirens, and in haron, the dark
ferryman of ades. he descendants of the omans and truscans hold
that the old truscan gods and the oman deities of the woods and fields
still live in the world as spirits. he high altars of the â€ord of
the oundâ€ and his terrible kin were levelled, and their golden images
and great temples left to moulder in abandonment but the rude rustic
shrine to the rude rustic god still received its offerings. It is this
shifting of the care of the pagan cult from chief to peasant, from court
to hovel, and, perhaps, to some extent from higher to lower race, that
serves to explain how the more primitive and uncouth gods have tended so
largely to supplant those of higher, more graceful mien. Aboriginal
deities, thrust into obscurity by the invasion of higher foreign types,
came back to their own again.

or it seems plain that we must divide the spiritual population of the
British Islands into two classes. here is little in common between the
â€fairyâ€, strictly socalled, and the unsightly elf who appears under
various names and guises, as pooka, leprechaun, brownie, knocker, or
bogle. he one belongs to such divine tribes as the uatha  anann of
aelic myth or their kin, the British gods of the abinogion. he other
owes his origin to a quite different, and much lower, kind of
imagination. One might fancy that neolithic man made him in his own
image.

one the less has immemorial tradition wonderfully preserved the
essential features of the eltic naturegods. he fairy belief of the
present day hardly differs at all from the conception which the elts
had of their deities. he description of the uatha  anann in the
â€ialogue of the ldersâ€ as â€sprites or fairies with corporeal or
material forms but indued with immortalityâ€ would stand as an account of
prevailing ideas as to the â€good peopleâ€ today. or do the Irish and
Welsh fairies of popular belief differ from one another. Both alike live
among the hills, though in Wales a lake often takes the place of the
â€fairy moundâ€ both, though they war and marry among themselves, are
semiimmortal both covet the children of men, and will steal them from
the cradle, leaving one of their own uncanny brood in the mortal babyâ€s
stead both can lay men and women under spells both delight in music
and the dance, and live lives of unreal and fantastic splendour and
luxury. Another point in which they resemble one another is in their
tiny size. But this would seem to be the result of the literary
convention originated by hakespeare in genuine folktales, both aelic
and British, the fairies are pictured as of at least mortal
stature.

But, Aryan or Iberian, beautiful or hideous, they are fast vanishing
from belief. very year, the secluded valleys in which men and women
might still live in the old way, and dream the old dreams, tend more and
more to be thrown open to the modern world of rapid movement and rapid
thought. he last ten years have perhaps done more in this direction
than the preceding ten generations. What lone shepherd or fisherman will
ever see again the vision of the great anannn ave the stableboys of
today still any faith left in invarra Is wyn ap udd often thought
of in his own valleys of the aw and the edd It would be hard,
perhaps, to find a wholehearted believer even in his local pooka or
parish bogle.

It is the ritual observances of the old eltic faith which have better
weathered, and will longer survive, the disintegrating influences of
time. here are no hard names to be remembered. hings may still be done
for â€luckâ€ which were once done for religion. ustomary observances die
very slowly, held up by an only half acknowledged fear that, unless they
are fulfilled, â€something may happenâ€. We shall get, therefore, more
satisfactory evidence of the nature of the eltic paganism by examining
such customs than in any other way.

We find three forms of the survival of the ancient religion into quite
recent times. he first is the celebration of the old solar or
agricultural festivals of the spring and autumn equinoxes and of the
summer and winter solstices. he second is the practice of a symbolic
human sacrifice by those who have forgotten its meaning, and only know
that they are keeping up an old custom, oined with late instances of
the actual sacrifices of animals to avert cattleplagues or to change
bad luck. he third consists of many stillliving relics of the once
universal worship of sacred waters, trees, stones, and animals.

Whatever may have been the exact meaning of the eltic state worship,
there seems to be no doubt that it centred around the four great days in
the year which chronicle the rise, progress, and decline of the sun,
and, therefore, of the fruits of the earth. hese were Beltaine, which
fell at the beginning of ay idsummer ay, marking the triumph of
sunshine and vegetation the east of ugh, when, in August, the
turningpoint of the sunâ€s course had been reached and the sad amhain,
when he bade farewell to power, and fell again for half a year under the
sway of the evil forces of winter and darkness.

Of these great solar periods, the first and the last were, naturally,
the most important. he whole eltic mythology seems to revolve upon
them, as upon pivots. It was on the day of Beltaine that artholon and
his people, the discoverers, and, indeed, the makers of Ireland, arrived
there from the other world, and it was on the same day, three hundred
years later, that they returned whence they came. It was on Beltaineday
that the aelic gods, the uatha  anann, and, after them, the aelic
men, first set foot on Irish soil. It was on the day of amhain that the
omors oppressed the people of emed with their terrible tax and it was
again at amhain that a later race of gods of light and life finally
conquered those demons at the Battle of oytura. Only one important
mythological incidentâ€and that was one added at a later timeâ€happened
upon any other than one of those two days it was upon idsummer ay,
one of the lesser solar points, that the people of the goddess anu took
Ireland from its inhabitants, the ir Bolgs.

he mythology of Britain preserves the same rootidea as that of
Ireland. If anything uncanny took place, it was sure to be on ayday.
It was on â€the night of the first of ayâ€ that hiannon lost, and

it was when she was â€amayingâ€ in the woods and fields near Westminster

he nature of the rites performed upon these days can be surmised from

hakespeare tells us that in his time the festival was so eagerly

cotland, â€auinâ€ in an, and â€os alangaeofâ€ (the ight of the Winter

omens for the future might be extorted from dark powers by uncanny

he sungod himself is said to have instituted the August festival

he characteristic features of these festivals give certain proof of
the original nature of the great pagan ceremonials of which they are
the survivals and travesties. In all of them, bonfires are
lighted on the highest hills, and the hearth fires solemnly rekindled.

and one must be careful to omit no prescribed rite, if one would avoid

comparatively late period. When ennant wrote, in the eighteenth

In their original form, these now harmless rural holidays were
undoubtedly religious festivals of an orgiastic natureworship such as
became so popular in reece in connection with the cult of ionysus. he

he bonfires which fill so large a part in the modern festivals have

flames. At the midsummer bonfires in Ireland all passed through the
fire the men when the flames were highest, the women when they were
lower, and the cattle when there was nothing left but smoke. In Wales,
upon the last day of October, the old amhain, there was a slightly
different, and still more suggestive rite. he hilltop bonfires were
watched until they were announced to be extinct. hen all would race
headlong down the hill, shouting a formula to the effect that the devil
would get the hindmost. he devil of a new belief is the god of the one
it has supplanted in all three instances, the custom was no mere
meaningless horseplay, but a symbolical human sacrifice.

A similar observance, but of a more cruel kind, was kept up in rance
upon t. ohnâ€s ay, until forbidden by law in the reign of ouis the

burned upon the bonfires, under the auspices and in the presence of the
sheriffs or the mayor of the town. aesar noted the custom among
the druids of constructing huge wickerwork images, which they filled
with living men, and set on fire, and it can hardly be doubted that the
wretched wolves, foxes, and cats were ceremonial substitutes for human
beings.

An ingenious theory was invented, after the introduction of
hristianity, with the purpose of allowing such ancient rites to
continue, with a changed meaning. he passing of persons and cattle
through flame or smoke was explained as a practice which interposed a
magic protection between them and the powers of evil. his homopathic
device of using the evil powerâ€s own sacred fire as a means of
protection against himself somewhat suggests that seething of the kid in
its motherâ€s milk which was reprobated by the evitical law but, no
doubt, pagan â€demonsâ€ were considered fair game. he explanation, of

druidical philosophy thatâ€to quote aesarâ€â€unless the life of man was
repaid for the life of man, the will of the immortal gods could not be
appeasedâ€ that dictated both the national and the private human
sacrifices of the elts, the shadows of which remain in the leaping
through the bonfires, and in the numerous recorded sacrifices of cattle
within quite recent times.

r. aurence omme, in his thnology in olklore, has collected many

he third form of eltic pagan survival is found in numerous instances

â€Aryanâ€ nations, the elts worshipped their rivers. he ee received

It underlies the three great worldreligions, and still forms the real
belief of perhaps the maority of their titular adherents. It is

t. tephenâ€s ay in Ireland. Its crime is said to be that it has â€a
drop of the deâ€ilâ€s blood in itâ€, but the real reason is probably to be
found in the fact that the Irish druids used to draw auguries from its
chirpings.

                                              

We have made in this volume some attempt to draw a picture of the
ancient religion of our earliest ancestors, the aelic and the British
elts. We have shown what can be gathered of the broken remnants of a
mythology as splendid in conception and as brilliant in colour as that
of the reeks. We have tried to paint its divine figures, and to retell
their heroic stories. We have seen them fall from their shrines, and
yet, rising again, take on new lives as kings, or saints, or knights of
romance, and we have caught fading glimpses of them surviving today as
the â€fairiesâ€, their rites still cherished by worshippers who hardly
know who or why they worship. Of necessity this survey has been brief
and incomplete. Whether the great edifice of the eltic mythology will
ever be wholly restored one can at present only speculate. Its colossal
fragments are perhaps too deeply buried and too widely scattered. But,
even as it stands ruined, it is a mighty quarry from which poets yet
unborn will hew spiritual marble for houses not made with hands.
